SUPERINTENDENT Of RED CROSS AT MANILA
SUSTAINS REPORT Of THE GOVERNMENT AGENT
Met Him While He Was Investigating the Condition of Affairs in the Philippines, Furnished Him With Data
and Knows That an Official Report Was Recently Made and Filed With
the War Department.
OSWALD H. J. SCHLOTT, who was the superintendent of the Red Cross Society at Man
ila, and who recently returned to this city, made the following statement last night
regarding the Government agent's report on conditions in the Philippines printed in The
Call yesterday:
My official position gave me exceptional opportunities for ascertaining the condition
of affairs in the islands. I traveled for four months visiting all the large islands, having in
my possession a pass from the insurgent government, by whom I was always received with
the utmost courtesy and respect.
In my travels I met the special agent of the Government, whom I know to have had
a pass from President McKinley which entitled him to special consideration from General Otis.
I found him to be a thorough gentleman and know he is of high standing in the United States.
He was entirely unprejudiced in making his investigations, and exercised his best endeav
ors to do justice to the army, the people of the United States, as well as the people in
habiting the archipelago.
I met the gentleman on his routes in the southern provinces of the Philippines, and
a portion of the statement made by him was received through me. He carried on his in
vestigations according to the instructions of the home Government and embodied the result
in the official report recently filed in Washington.
I know the article as published in The Call of Friday morning to be a copy of this offi
cial report made by him to the Assistant Secretary of War.
I do not know where The Call obtained this report, but I do know that such a report
was made and is on file in the War Department."
l Mail further says that the Free

The notification came to the State De
partment in the shape of a note from Mr.

here. The details of the transfer of Brit
ish interests In case of war had been pre

This officer Is the superior in rank to the
other Consular representatives of the
United States, not only in the Transvaal
but In the Orange Free State, and he has
been Instructed to give those officials the
necessary directions. The only other Con
sular officer besiues Mr. Macrum in the
South African Republic is Mr. Gordon,
who succeeded Mr. Manion as Consular
agent at Johannesburg. Mr. Manion hav
ing resigned a few months ago. In the
Orange Free State the United States is
represented by Alfred Elliott, Consular
agent at Bloomfontein. He Is an English
man, and therefore it is questionable
whether he will remain at his post In his
capacity as American agent or retire. In
the latter case Mr. Macrum will probably
select some suitable American to take up
the duties of Consular agent.
There is no present Intention at the
State Department to issue a proclamation
of neutrality. It Is customary to omit
these proclamations ' until . some emer
gency arises calling for their Issue, and
such an emergency Is not expected to oc
cur In South Africa.

BRITONS BEGIN
TO REALIZE THAT
THE WAR IS ON
LONDON. Oct. 13.-The announcement
that, the Boers had destroyed an armored
train on the western border of the Trans
vaal is. calculated to bring home to "the
man in the street" the realities of war.
Attempts are made to liken the occur
rence to the blowing up of the United
States battleship Maine in Havana har
bor, but that event occurred in times of
peace. The Boers are doubtless within
their rights as belligerents if they are
responsible for the destruction of the
train. '.'="â ' 'v.*. ': xlr-i:". ... .
Apart from : this destruction of the
.. armored, train, the most notable change
| in the position of affairs is the presence of
i Boers at Maribogo, forty miles south of
j Mafeking, which seems to indicate that
they are endeavoring . to get . Colonel
i Baden-Powell between two fires.
. The gravity of the Boer advance can be
better estimated when it is realized that
they will thereby cut railway and tele
graphic communication to the north
isolating several . British positions which
must be speedily relieved.
Despite the optimistic . reports of the
ability of Mafeking to repel attack, the
greatest anxiety prevails here regarding
the situation . there. . as It is known that
the redoubtable commandant, Cronje, who
captured the Jameson raiders, has the
strongest force yet put in the field with
the exception of Commandant General
Joubert's force. Cronje's troops now
number between 9000 and 10,000 men.
The position in Natal has not materially
' altered. The reported inroad of 3000 Boers
by way of Tintwa Pass is probably identi
cal with the inroad of the force reported
yesterday as having crossed Van Reenans
Pass. The Ttntwa column, according to
the last advices, had reached .within twen
ty miles of Ladysmith. On that showing
there ought to be speedy news of fighting.
Ladysmith is at present the Aldershot of
Natal.
A dispatch from Cape Town says that
no doubt -is felt as to the loyalty of
Lerothidi and other native chiefs, and the
Basutos are still well in hand, but the
Resident Commissioner has decided to pa
trol the border in order to prevent raids.

NEW FRATERNITY
HOUSE AT STANFORD
HANDSOME BUILDING TO BE ERECTED
BY THE KAPPA KAPPA GAMM.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY. Oct. 13.â The Kappa Kappa Gamma Sorosls of
Stanford University has let the contracts for the erection of an elegant
new house on Lasuen street, on the campus. Work will be begun Im
mediately and the house will be ready for occupancy early next year.
The cost will bo in the neighborhood of $7000. ' The Kappas have the dis
tinction of being the first sorosls to build a home at Stanford. *
The new Kappa house will be one of the handsomest of the group of
fraternity houses on the campus. It is designed in the old English style of
architecture, with steep, sloping roofs and broad porches. On tha lawn floor
will be a spacious reception hall, with the dining-room back of it and the
parlors to one side.

JOUBERT SAYS
BOERS MUST NOT :
ACT LIKE ROBBERS
PRETORIA, Oct. 12 (delayed in trans
mission).âCommandant General Joubert's
command Is now at Volksrust. Several
Cape Colonists have been sworn in as
burghers. Postal communication with the
surrounding colonies has been suspended,
but that between the Transvaal and the
Orange Free ate is maintained. Treas
urer General de Villiers and Mr. Vander
post of the Free State Raad are now here
consulting with the Government. The
Minister of Mines reports that a satis
factory number of mines are being
worked and that the Government will
keep the shafts of abandoned mines free
of water in the interest of shareholders.
It is reported that forty-six cases of
gold valued at $500,000. which were dis
covered In different canteens in Johan
nesburg, have been confiscated oy the
Government,
â¢ Commandant General. Joubert has Is
sued a circular from the chief laager in
consequence of the report that some of
the burghers had misbehaved themselves
on their journey to the borders, plunder
ing a number of -stores. Such offenses,
the commandant general says, will be se
verely punished. "When we are unwil
lingly compelled to cross the boundary
line of our country," says General Jou
bert, "let it not be thought that we are a
band of robbers and with that in view re
main as far as possible from private
dwellings and from places where no ene
emy Is stationed. When food for forage
for the cattle is needed let certain officers
acquire such goods from the owner and
let a receipt be given with promise of
recompense by the Government."
The Volkstein says: Two small repub
lics have entered upon a struggle with a
gigantic power which wishes to crush
them. Conquer or die is the battle cry
of the brave burghers. Thus will .he
question be solved and the republics once
for all will either be freed from provoca
tion'or will have to bend their net ks to
the yokes of British tyranny. England's
might will not decide the issue. That
rests with God alone.
BOER ATTACK EXPECTED
CAPE TOWN. Oct. 12. evening (Delayed
in transmission).â A dispatch from Mafe
king dated 3:22 o'clock this afternoon says
it is officially stated that the enemy will
probably roach this afternoon or to
morrow. Parties will be sent to watt on
all sides, and guns will be posted on the
high ground southeast of the town. It is
likely the enemy will shell the town for
some hours before attempting to take it
by assault. Already the Boers complain.
They want to attend to their crops, and
many are deserting. Meat is scarce, and
dysentery is prevalent. If the garrison
does nothing- it is probable that the Boers
will retire, giving the garrison, in the
mean opportunities' for hitting them
hard. The garrison must seize and utilize
such opportunities.
Information has been received from an
other source that the Boers have actually
crossed the frontier some miles south of
the town. This information is believed to
be correct though it lacks confirmation.
PREMIER SCHREINER'S PLEA.
CAPE TOWN, Oct. 13.â Premier Schrel
ner, on moving the adjournment of the
Cape House of Assembly, made a nota
ble speech, in the course of which he al
luded to the position in which the Cape
Government was placed. He said he
would do the duty Imposed upon him
without favor or fear â the duty of all
to save the colony, so far as possible,
from being involved in the vortex of war.
In his circulars to the military com
manders he had already given his reasons
for hoping that the people would remain
quiet, and he would appeal to the mem
bers of the Legislature to possess their
souls In patience, however dark the days
might be, and wait with quietness and
confidence. To the rress representing both
sides in- would appeal for pity, sympathy,
generosity and charity; and lie wished
to make a similar appeal to the churches
and to the natives of the colony. The time
had not yet arrived for him to say what
part he had played In trying to estab
lish peace in South Africa, That would
come later.
"Let this colony," said the Premier in
conclusion, "be a kind haven during the
storm which has burst upon South Af
rica, and let us all pray for the re-estab
lishment of honorable peace throughout
our beloved country."

AN APPEAL TO AFRIKANDERS.
PRETORIA, Oct. 12, via Lourenzo
Marques, Oct. 13.â An official manifesto
has Just been addressed to Afrikanders
throughout South Africa appealing to
them to resist "the unjust demands of
Great Britain" and accusing Lord Salis
bury. Mr. Chamberlain and Sir Alfred
Milner of treachery. It blames the Queen
for condoning what it describes as "Mr.
Chamberlain's criminal policy," and de
clares "that the clear desire and object
of Great Britain are to deprive the Trans
vaal of independence on account of the
gold mines of the Rand."
- The manifesto says that "Great Britain
has offered two alternatives five-year
franchise and war"â and then goes on to
say: . . â¢
"A difference between the two Govern
ments of two years on the franchise ques
tion is considered by her Majesty's Gov-*
ernment sufficient justification for an en
deavor to swallow the two republics."
In conclusion it reminds Afrikanders
that "Gcd will assuredly defend the
right."
The manifesto is signed by Mr. Reitz,
Secretary of State. A million copies will
bo printed In Dutch and English.

ENGLISHMAN MURDERED
BY FRENCH DETECTIVES
Set Upon Because His Master Had
Incurred the Wrath of Officials
at Dieppe.
NEW YORK, Oct. 14.â A special to the
Times from Paris says: A great sensa
tion has been caused here by the murder
of an Englishman named Derrick by a
French detective at Dieppe. Derrick was
so shockingly injured that an unusual sur
gical ration had to be performed upon
him, with the result that gangrene Inter
vened and he died.
Derrick was set upon nt the same time
as his master, a wealthy Englishman
named Captain O'Neill Murphy, who had
incurred trie wrath of Dieppe Casino offi
cers because he had caused the director
of Petits Chevaux at Calais to be con
demned for swindling.
Captain Murphy is one of the most re
spected English residents of Paris; he is
a brother of D. F. Murphy of San Fran
cisco. In spite of his passport and _cre
dentials he was hauled off to jail, stripped
and measured and finally sentenced to
eight days' imprisonment for protecting
his own house.
The British Consul is attempting to
bring the murderers of Derrick to justice.
English feeling is intensely excited over
the incident, which is likely to have im
portant International consequences.

ANOTHER FIRE IN
THE BUSH STORES
Warehouse No. 3 Destroyed, Bring
ing the Loss Up to a Half
Million.
NEW YORK. Oct. 14.â At 2:30 o'clock
this morning fire broke out in the build
ings of the Bush Independent Stores,
which were partially destroyed by fire
yesterday afternoon. The fire this morn
ing apparently started in building No. 2,
which was separated from No. 3 by a
brick wall two feet in thickness.
No. 3 was totally destroyed yesterday,
and it. is thought at this hour, 2:30 a. m.,
that building No. 2 will also prove a total
loss. Building No. 2 contained about
10.000 bales of cotton valued at from $250,
--000 to $300,000. Three firemen were over
come by smoke, and the fire Is not yet
under control. The officials of the. Bush
Independent Stores estimate that the total
loss in consequence of the fire in both
warehouses will certainly exceed $500,000,
Warehouse No. 1 was destroyed by lire a
few months aero.
MAY HAVE BEEN MURDERED.
Body of a Chinaman Found in the
Bay.
SAN RAFAEL, Oct. 13. -The body of a
Chinese was found floating In the bay
near the railway wharf at Sausalito to
day, by Christian Ock, a longshoreman.
Coroner Eden was ' notified and brought
the remains to this city. A cursory exam
ination showed that death had probably
resulted from a bullet wound under the
left ear, as there was no water in the
lungs, though the body had been In the
bay about two weeks.
This evening a Chinese named Wong
How called at the Morgue in company
with two of his countrymen and identified
the dead man as his partner, Lee Doo
Teong, who he claimed was killed a
week ago last Tuesday by the Jibboom of
a fishing smack knocking him into the
waters of the bay at Point San Pedro.
When questioned about the bullet wound
and absence of water in the lungs, the
Chinese became suspiciously silent. The
inquest will occur to-morrow.
About 200 Chinese live at. Point San Pe
dro, and their fishing camp there has
gained a very hard name. Many men
have disappeared from there and have
never been heard of again, while brawls
and cutting affairs are of common occur
rence. It is a hiding-place for highbind
ers when the police make it too interest
ing for them in San Francisco, and once
ensconced in the camp it is almost impos
sible for the authorities to locate a crim
inal.

Â» â¢
PROSPECTS OF CONTINUANCE
* â
HERDS OF CATTLE NEAR
TRUCKEE ENDANGERED.
Â»
Damage Done in Some Sections, but
as a Rule the Farmers Are
Very Grateful for the
Downpour.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TRUCKEE, Oct. 13.â For three days
snow has fallen almost continuously. No
such storm has occurred in October since
IMG. when the Dormer party were snow
bound at Dormer Lake. Ono of Truckee's
principal Interests is the pasturing of
sheep and cattle during the summer
months. All the stock and herds are still
in the mountains, and the greatest con
sternation prevails lest they perish, from
hunger and exposure. Most of the snow
has melted as it fell, but there is one foot
on the ground at Truckee, and from two
to four feet in the surrounding moun
tains. The storm was a complete surprise
and the cattle are scattered all over the
mountains.
Some of the dairymen are hauling hay
| to their stock, but the supply is limited,
| and thousands of cattle and sheep must
perish soon if the storm continues.
LONG BEACH, Oct. 13.-One of the
worst storms that ever visited this re
gion is now raging. There is a howling
south wind and a rough sea. The float
at the end of the pleasure wharf was
blown ashore, destroying a portion of the
wharf. The sailboat Elk dragged her an
chor and was smashed to splinters in the
surf. Other small craft have been dam
aged. Rain is falling steadily. The wind
is increasing in velocity.
FRESNO, Oct. 13.â cyclone passed
over Fresno to-day and a high wind un
derneath did considerable damage.
Weather Observer Bolton says nothing
like it was ever seen in California before.
SACRAMENTO, Oct. 13.-Last night the
Sacramento Valley had a thorough
drenching. Rain fell almost steadily dur
ing the entire night and up to nearly 10
o'clock tins morning. it was one of the
heaviest October storms the valley lias
had in a number of years. Rain was
badly needed, particularly from a sani
tary point of view, as there is considera
ble sickness throughout the valley. Some
grain which was not housed will be dam
aged. There will also be slight damage
to grapes. -
Tnere was a heavy fall of snow in the
mountains last night along the Central
Pacific road, and snow has been tailing
all day at Summit. Snow is already over
tine, feet in depth. Railroad traffic has
not been interfered with.
The Weather Bureau reports show the
rainfall las', night and up to 9 o'clock this
morning to have been 1.18 inches, and for
this month 1.34 inches, while the season s
total precipitation is 1.36 inches, as against
.70 of an Inch to an equal date last year.

HERO OF THE
WAR ARRESTED
H. B. Ford Recognized
as a Fugitive.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SPOKANE, Oct, 13â Back from the
Philippines, where he made an enviable
record for bravery, came H. B. Ford of
Company B, Idaho Volunteers. With his
company he was royally welcomed by the
people of his own State. Then Ford came
to Spokane. He was on the streets but
a few hours before he was recognized by
Sheriff Tucker of Yakima County as a
fugitive from justice.
Six years ago Tucker had some trouble
with Ford, who was then a mere boy,
and Ford pulled a revolver and fired five
shots at Tucker. None took effect. Ford
was arrested, tried, convicted and sen
tenced to ten years' imprisonment. He
escaped while his case was being ap
pealed. He assumed the name of Ford,
his right name being William Feamster.
For six years he dodged the officers of
the law. Now he is glad he was recap
tured, for Sheriff Tucker and Judge
Graves, who tried and sentenced Ford,
have labored on his behalf and the Su
perior Court has suspended sentence per
manently. Ford's valor as a soldier has
relieved him from punishment for his
youthful crime. He again bears the name
of Feamster and liberty is his.

SUGAR PLANTERS COMPLAIN.
Steamship Service Not Adequate at
Peruvian Ports.
Special Cable to The Call and the New Tork
Herald. Copyrighted, 1599. by James Gor-
don Bennett.
LIMA, Peru, Oct. 13.â Sugar planters in
the north are complaining bitterly of the
irregular service of the Pacific Steam
Navigation Company along the coast.
More than four thousand tons of sugar
are lying in Salavery alone to-day await
ing shipment. In another port one estate
has more than 3000 tons waiting for a
steamship. The planters declare this de
lay Is simply ruinous.
In his annual report to his Government
the Belgian Consul in Lima denounces as
an abuse the practice of the Postoffice
authorities In charging on correspondence
for the United States and Europe via
Havana 10 per cent more than the stipu
lated postal union rate.

LAGOS, East Africa, Oct. 13.â The British punitive expedition against the

REA PROMISES
A SETTLEMENT
Meeting of Union Bank
Stockholders.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, Oct. There was a lively
meeting of the stockholders of the de- |
funct Union Savings Bank at Turn Verein ,
Hall this morning. For a time it looked j
as though the session might break up .n |
a row. Senator Shortridge and A. Bar
ker got into neated dispute, in which
the words thief and liar were freely used,
but not directly, but ex-Boss Rea wound j
things up in a suave manner, in which he j
eulogized ex-President Ward Wright and
Will H. Wright, saying the former was
an honest and upright man until E. B. I
Howard, the fruit-shipper, had got him
in his clutches. The directors were asked
to levy an assessment of $60 per share
in order to pay the depositors in full.
The meeting of stockholders was culled
by President Rea for the express purpose
of presenting his first semi-annual ac
count of his management of the bank and
to outline a future course of action. On
the stage beside him were Manager Aus
tin and Secretary A. Friant. About two
hundred stockholders and depositors were
present.
After calling the meeting to order Presi
dent Rea read his semi-annual account,
and then made an explanatory statement.
He said when all the assets were real
ized on there would still be a deficiency
of $277,897 61. In order to pay the DOS
itors in full he favored an assessment of
$60 or $70 per share. An assessment of $.0
per share would realize from solvent
stockholders $310,971, and $70 per share
would bring in $373,299. This was with an
allowance that only a little over 50 per
cent of stockholders could be made to
pay the assessment. He stated he had
not received the entire support of the
board of directors in his management, but
Peter Stock had resigned, and he had had
T. B. Caldwell elected a director. With
the aid of the latter and Directors Dun
lop and Simpson he now proposed to pro
ceed to a settlement of the bank's affairs
as speedily as possible. Directors Eaton,
Jones and Callisch had opposed him in
various matters. They had refused to pay
the $10 assessment recently levied on the
stock and were fighting the same, with
a view of postponing its payment until
time had elapsed when further assess
ments could be levied. He promised to
see that all were made to pay the assess
ment, and that depositors should receive
100 cents on the dollar.
One of the stockholders inquired If Rea
did not owe the bank some money. The
president replied that he did owe the bank
$5000, but it was amply secured.
Senator Shortridge then got the floor
and monopolized it pretty much the rest
of the meeting. -In fact he became a
dictator. He said he had. been robbed
both as a depositor and a stockholder.
Shortridge said stockholders who could
and would not pay. the assessment were
thieves. Howard was denounced as a.
thief and the man -responsible for break
ing the bank. He wanted all the stock
holders who refused to pay a $60 assess
ment sued.
It was then developed that shortly be
fore the failure when stockholders kicked
on the management of the bank the pres
ident had a practice of buying their stock.
A motion was made that an assessment
of $60 per share be levied in order to pay
the depositors in full. This passed, about
half a dozen voting in favor of it. A $10
assessment has already been levied.
President Rea stated that in the course
of a couple of weeks he would call a
meeting of the directors for levying such
an assessment.
A motion to appoint a committee of five
to meet a similar committee of depositors
to see if a settlement could not be ef
fected at less than the value of deposits
was lost, Shortridge and half a dozen
friends voting the matter down.
Aside from asking for a $60 assessment,
on which only a few voted, the meeting
was in the nature of a Shortridge-Rea
admiration society, and had the appear
ance of being called for the purpose of
clearing the skirts of the Wrights for all
blame in the wreck of the bank.
The board of directors met this after
noon, but transacted nothing but routine
business. Amat>a Eaton was elected vice
president of the bank.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
FARGO. N. D., Oct. 13.-Over 10.000 per
sons greeted President McKinley and his
party when they arrived here to-night at
7:30. Entering carriages the party was
driven to a reviewing stand through a
line of the First North Dakota Volun
teers, who recently returned from the
Philippines. The President was received
with wild demonstrations. In his address
he referred repeatedly to the situation in
the Philippines. When he declared the
"United States Government would send
60,000 men to the Philippines to uphold the
American flag, where the volunteers had
placed it, the crowd approved the declara
tion with wild applause. The President
said in part:
I have come here especially that I might look
Into the taces of the North Dakota Volunteers,
who saw service ln the battle line in Luzon.
You did your duty and you tilled my heart with
joy when with other volunteers and regulars,
you sent me word that you would not quit the
battle line in Luzon '.11 could create a new
army and send it there. You refused to beat
a re'trert or shirk your colors in the presence
of the enemy. No matter who wanted you to
Â£o home you said. "We will stay and keep^the
tiag stainless in the presence of the enemy."
No soldiers cf any country ever had any more
delicate or trying 'duty. This army, of which
this fragment from your State formed a part,
remained in Luzon waiting, first for the treaty
of peace, which was being perpared in Paris,
then for its ratification by the Senate of the
United States, then until the exchange of
ratification between the Unit States and
Spainâ waiting through all that long period,
accepting the insolence of the insurgents with
dignity, because they were ordered by the ex
ecutive of the nation not to strike a blow pend
ing the treaty of peace, except in defense. They
accepted; therefore, the insolence of the Insur
gents: with a patience sublime.
We never dreamed that the little body of In
surgents whom we have Just emancipated from
Spain would turn upon the flag that had de
livered them from Siialn. Then it was that
the insurgent leader began an attack upon
our men and then our boys let loose. But I
always thought the boys were quite ready for
It. (Applause.; I want to give them the ere lit
of saying here in the presence of their neigh
bors ami friends, their fathers -.'! mothers,
that these boys forebore all things rather than
disobey the order of the Government they were
Eervingr. The leader of the insurgent " forces
lays to the American Government: "You can
have peace if you will give us Independence."
Peace for independence, he says. He had an
other price than that for peace once before,
but the United States ays no gold for peace.
We never gave a hribp for peace in all our
history and we never will. (Applause.)
Ourj flag is there where you left it. you >.'.>Â«

FIRST TERRITORIAL
CONVENTION IN ALASKA
Opposes the Ceding to Great Britain
of Any Territory Acquired
From Russia.
SEATTLE. Wash., Oct. 13. -A special to
the Post-Intelligencer from Juneau. Alas
ka, says: Alaska's first Territorial Con
vention, attended by delegates from every
section of the Territory, was called to
order in the opera-house' on October 10,
and will spend two weeks in preparing for \
a presentation to the next Congress Alas
ka's needs In the way of legislation.
Ex-Governor A. P. Swineford was elect
ed permanent chairman and Al Hoffman
of Juneau secretary. Resolutions present
ed by Judge K. Delancy of Juneau were
adopted, expressing the uncompromising '
opposition of the convention to the sur
render to Great Britain in any manner of
any territory acquired by the United
States from Russia and calling upon
! President McKinley and the authorities
at Washington to firmly and steadfastly
resist all attempts, however insidious, of
; any foreign power for the dismemberment
!of Alaska. A copy of the resolutions was
forwarded to President McKinley.
j During to-day's session a letter was read
from Senator Foster of Washington, writ
ten subsequent to his visit to this Terri
tory last summer, which was received
with manifestations of approval and en
tered ln full upon the minutes. Standing ;
committees on all important branches of
the convention's proposed work were ap
pointed. . They will do most of the work :
in the committee room. The largest dele
gations are from Douglas, Skaguay and
Juneau.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, OCTOBER 14, 1899,
NORTHWESTERN TRAIN
ROBBED NEAR CHICAGO
Five Men Board a Limited Express, Blow Open and
Loot the Safe and Escape Hotly Pursued
by Officers From the City.
Volleys of Bullets Are Sped Toward the Crew, and the Engineer Is
Wounded While Resisting the Demands of the Outlaws That He
Run His Locomotive Away From the Train.
CHICAGO, Oct. 14.â Masked robbers
held up train No. 9 of the Northwestern
Railroad shortly before midnight between
Maple Park and De Kalb and shot at Dan
White, the engineer, and ran away with
and blew open the express car and safe.
When the robbers had rifled the car they
disappeared. '.:';â¢';.; ',
Train No. 9 forms part of the fast trans
continental mail, which was recently es
: tablished between New York and San
j Francisco. The run is made from Chicago
i to Council Bluffs on a schedule exceed
ing a mile a minute. It has been the gen
eral custom to carry only two cars, one
containing express matter and the other
! mail.
Train No. 9 left the Northwestern depot
at 10 o'clock, with Dan White, a veteran
I employe of the road, as engineer. No
! stops were scheduled before De Kalb, at
! which place the train was due shortly
before 11 o'clock. At Maple Park, fifty
miles from Chicago, It was discovered
I that tower "W," at Elburn, was either
out of order or was not giving the right
signal. Dan White closed the throttle
quickly and brought the train to a stand
still.
j The train was midway between Elburn
i and Maple Park. White had hardly
brought his engine to a standstill when
two masked men jumped on the steps and
i pointed guns at him and the fireman.
"Throw up your hands:" they shouted.
1 The railroad men did so.
Two robbers detached the engine from
. the rest of the train and White was told
jto pull out. The robbers ordered him to
i take his engine two miles up the track.
, There he attempted to make a fight and
| recapture his engine. . He struggled man
: fully, but finally one of the robbers shot
at him, but failed to hit him.
In the meantime four other men had
attacked the conductor and the brakeman.
; Numerous shots were fired to intimidate

Company's wharf, and will hereafter
carry on an extensive fish smoking and
packing business. He will euro sardines
chiefly, although mackerel and other
kinds of fish will also be put up. . Monte
rev Bay affords large quantities of sar
dines at all seasons of the year, and there
Is no doubt that the industry will prove
a lucrative one for its owner and will also
give employment to many hands.
SUBTERRANEAN LAKE IN
HEART OF A MOUNTAIN
Discovery Made by a Party of Pros
pectors, in British
Columbia.
VICTORIA, B. C, Oct. 13.â discov
ery has been made at Goldstream, within
twenty miles of Victoria, of a large sub
terranean lake in the heart of Mount
Skirt which attains an altitude of 1100
feet above the level of the sea. There are
a number of gold and copper properties
there, all of which promise to be very
rich. The find was made by a party con
sisting of P. H. and Chris Peterson and
A. G. West, all of this city. They had a
partner. W. A. Saunders, who has just
returned from Alaska, and he will leave
by the next steamer for San Francisco
to endeavor to interest capitalists. The
three men were following a rich ledge
of copper ore between fifteen and twenty
feet wide, found by them on the moun
tain. In going about this work they
found a natural shaft sinking at an
angle in the very center of the ledge and
decided to explore It.
About twenty feet from the surface
Peterson found the passage open out Into
a large chamber. Candles were lighted
and the men found that they stood on a
narrow shelving platform of quartz, while
Stretching away as far as they could see
lay a lake which proved to be of fresh
water of icy coldness. The depth of the
water could not be fathomed. Seven or
eight feet above their heads extended a
roof of blackened rock, from which were
suspended Icicle-shaped pendants, lack
ing, however, the brilliancy of stalactites.
interesting as the phenomenon may be
on its own merits it will also be of great
advantage in exploiting the ledge. By
installing a siphon the lake can easily
be emptied, even though of great depth.
This will leave an Immense tunnel, which
will greatly facilitate the work of stoping
and will form an ideal tunnel for explora
tion.
MUST GO TO SCHOOL.
State Truant Law Will Be Enforced
in Pasadena.
PASADENA. Oct. 13.â Steps have been
taken by the School Trustees of Pasadena
to enforce the State truant law, which re
quires of parents or guardians that chil
dren between the ages of 8 and 14 years
be sent to school during at. least two
thirds of the school year, unless excused
by the Board of Education because of
physical disability, poverty or attendance
at private schools. lasts are in prepara
tion by the principals of the seven schools
which will contain the names of all chil
dren in the seven districts who should be
in school, but are not. These lists will
be given to Marshal Lacey, who will no
tify the parents and guardians.
In case the" law is not compiled with
after notice has been served, the Board
of Trustees will prosecute. The penalty
is a maximum fine of $20 for th.. first of
fense and between $20 and (60 for subse
quent offenses, which fines go to the
school funds when collected. There are
at present- ISSO pupils in the schools. More
than 100 truants will be brought In by thÂ«
enforcement of the law.

Injured by Dynamite.
SALINAS, Oct. Miss Annie Grove,
the 19-year-old daughter of a prominent
rancher of this valley, was Injured by an
explosion of dynamite to-day. The brother
of the girl found- three small tin boxes
and took â¢ them homo. His sister com
menced to pry off the cover if one of
them, when it exploded and lacerated her
horribly. Miss Grove will be disfigured
for life if she recovers.

LOS ANGELES. Oct. 13.-The Supreme
Court to-day affirmed the decision of the
Superior Court In the case of the city
of San Diego vs. the San Diego Water
Company. The suit arose over the
amount of money to be paid the water
company during the time the cltv was in
possession of the plant, and was" decided
in favor of the municipality.

press Company that there was $25,000 in

been In the car in cash alone. The amount
of jewelry, etc., Mr. Anrisdel . says he
could not state.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
NORTHFIELD, Vt., Oct. Admiral
Dewey to-day laid the cornerstone of
Dewey Mall, which is to be a part of
Norwich University, the institution in
which the admiral received his earliest
military training. I The admiral was es
corted from his train to the university
by the Norwich University cadets and
the First Regiment, Vermont National
Guard. Admiral Dewey spread the mortar
and as the cornerstone was lowered said:
"I now declare this stone duiy and truly
laid, and according to my wish."
Senator-elect Chauncey M. Depew of
New York delivered the address of the
day. At the conclusion of Mr. Depew's
address Admiral Dewey held a reception
for a short time
BOSTON, Oct. 13.â Admiral Dewey and
a small party of friends, Including his
son and Lieutenant Brumby, left North
field, Vt.. at 12:20 o'clock this afternoon
on a special train of two parlor cars.
At every station there was a cheering
crowd and occasionally a Grand Army
post would be drawn up In line. Salutes
were fired with torpedoes or with rifles.
At each station the train was slowed up
so that the people could see the admiral,
who sat at the .windows In .he rear car.
At White River Junction the train was
sent across the river to West Lebanon,
where the train of President Tuttle of
the Boston and Maine road was waiting,
containing besides the president and other
railroad officials Governor Frank W. Rol
lins and other New Hampshire officials,
and representatives of the Governor and
Legislature of Massachusetts. The car
in which the admiral rode was attached
to President Tuttle's train and Governor
Rollins, who was to conduct the admiral
through New Hampshire, was introduced
to his guest. By this time the crowd of
sightseers was cheering and the admiral
stepped upon the rear platform in time
to near the' Dartmouth boys from Han
over give the yell. Then they called far
a speech and the admiral said:
"I can't make a speech."
Just then the train started, the college
boys veiled and the admiral called out:
"That's it. boys; you'll own the country
some day."
As the train ran through New Hamp
shire the enthusiasm increased as the
crowds were larger. Factories along the
track blew their whistles, fire bells were
rung and torpedoes were continually ex
ploding along the track. At all the sta
tions the train ran slowly and the ad
miral and Governor Rollins stood together
on the platform, bowing right and left.
At Concord the crowd was a big one.
The train pulled down to the railroad
shops, where a change of engines was
made. The â¢ new. engine, labeled the
"Olympia," backed up to where the ad
miral could see the front. It bore an im
mense gilt medallion of the admiral on
the boiler head, while under it was a
miniature barbette with long guns and
the inscription, "Stand to your guns and
obey orders."
While the train was in waiting Admiral
Dewey, from the rear platform, was
greeted by several of St. Paul's school
boys and shook bands with several
friends. Then the train continued to
Manchester, reaching there amid a salute
from the First New Hampshire Battery.
Each window in the great Amoskeag cor
poration building facing the railroad had
Its knot of operatives waving cloth or
aprons. As the train passed from New
Hampshire to Massachusetts territory
the Massachusetts representatives went
Into the admiral's car and were Intro
duced by Governor Rollins, who turned
the guest over to the care of Massa
chusetts.
At Lowell the crowd was larger than
any vet seen. From Lowell to Boston it
seemed as if the tracks were lined with
people so continuous was the cheering.
The train pulled Into the union station
in this city at 6:05 p. m., to find there a
terrific jam of humanity. Fully 25,000
persons were crowded ln and around the
station, and in the rush to get a look at
th.- hero he was well nigh carried off his
foet. He was met by Mayor Quincy and
a delegation representing the Homo Mar
ket Club, the Chamber of Commerce and
other municipal bodies. The admiral rode
at the head of a column of 10,000 war
veterans through the city's principal
downtown streets to the Hotel Touralne,
which will be his home during his stay
of three days. The admiral's escort to
night were the naval veterans and fol
lowing them were posts of the Massachu
setts Department of the Grand Army and
Spanish war veterans. All the streets
were profusely decorated and crowds

The admiral, after dinner, witnessed a
fine display of fireworks on Boston Com
mon from his apartments in he hotel
and retired soon after. To-morrow he will
have an arduous day. as he will attend
many functions. In the forenoon he will
hear the school children sing on the
common, be presented a watch at City
Hall and review a parade which will In
clude the entire State militia, mobilized
for its fall maneuvers. In the evening
there will be a banquet at the Algonquin
Club. The admiral expects to leave for
New York on Sunday.
TAMASESE PROCLAIMED
KING OF SAMOA
Natives Choose Their Favorite Ruler
Despite the Late Agreement of the
Treaty Powers.
CHICAGO, Oct. 14.â A special cable to the Chicago Tribune from Berlin says:
News comes frcm Apia that the natives have proclaimed Tamasese as King
despite the recent agreement of the United States, Germany and Great Britain
to abolish that office.
The Cologne Gazette's Apia correspondent sends a long indictment of the
Consular Government. He says the natives do not conceal their disrespect for
the representatives of the treaty powers, relating a singular incident as an illus
tration. Tamasese Invited the officials and the leading white citizens of Apia
to a wedding feast. All went except the Germans.
The American Consul, Mr. Osborne, made a speech, thus giving the affair
official recognition. Thereupon Tamasese's followers danced and sang impromptu
songs proclaiming Tamasese as King and announcing the destruction of the
opposing natives if they resisted his authority.
When the letter was mailed, September 8 hundreds of Tamasese's and Tanu's
followers were gathered at Apia preparing to swear allegiance before the eyes of
the foreign Consuls by presenting gifts and attesting homage. The letter says a
New Zealand lawyer named Gurr is managing the affair.
COUNCIL DELIBERATIONS.
The Democratic Party's Platform Si-
lent on the Labor Question.
The silence of the Democratic party on
the labor question In its platform was
the cause of comment at the meeting of
the Labor Council last night. A dele
gate in speaking of the omission made
the statement that he had noticed that
the Republican party had placed a plank
In its platform favoring labor unions. In
order to be correct on the proposition,
and fearing that the entire platforms of j
the parties had not been carefully printed j
In the dally newspapers, he moved the
following resolution, which was adopted: i
Resolved, That the secretary be instructed to
obtain from the county committees of the vari
ous political parties bearing on municipal tick
ets in the field copies of planks In their plat
forms relating to organized labor and present
said copies to this council as early as prac
ticable.
The boycott on Techau's Tavern has
been extended to cover all parts of the
building. Heretofore it only applied to
the dining room, but now the entire
building has been blanketed. The Musi
clans' Union complained of the Danish
society Daniaand Danish Brotherhood of
Oakland for hiring "scab" music at . its
moonlight picnic which will be held on
the night of October 22. The Carpenters'
Union No. 36 of Oakland were notified of
this fact, several of Its members being
also members of the society.
A communication was received from
Samuel (Jompers, ln which it was stated
thet the Bricklayers' and Masons' appli
cation for affiliation to the A. F. of L.
would not be acted on before the regu
lar annual meeting in January next. He
found fault with the San Francisco trades
unions for not giving the bricklayers'
local union due recognition. This union,
lie said, was affiliated with the interna
tional union, and as such was entitled to
a respectful consideration.
The delegates from the Theatrical
State Employes' Union. W. G. Rusk and
Robert Little, were seated, as was A.
Foertsch from the Milkers' Union.
The Fresco and Paperhangers' organi
zation received its charter from the
Brotherhood of Painters and Decorators
of America, and was duly Installed last
night.
A decision from the Supreme Court of
Michigan indorsing the action of Judge
Mogan to a certain degree was hailed
with much delight. It is as follows:
Laborers have the. right to fix a price upon
their labor and to refuse work unless that
price Is obtained. Singly or In : combination
they have this right. They may present their
cause to the public in newspapers or circulars
in a peaceable way and with no attempt at
coercion. If the effect - of such a case is ruin
to the employer It Is damnum obosque Injuria,
for they have only exercised their legal rights!
At a meeting of the Building Trades
Council last night the plasterers' and
stalrbullders' delegates were admitted to
full membership.
The secretary was Instructed to com
municate with political county commit-
The P' ihppine War.
This contest is proving much more stubborn
than we anticipated. It needs a vigorous con
test to straighten matters out. We should
tackle the Philippines and overcome them as
Hosteller's Stomach Bitters does dyspepsia,
Indices' malaria, fever and ague and liver
and kidney diseases. The battle Is short and
decisive, and for fifty years the Bitters has
always won. If you are 111, and don't know
what's wrong, try it.

with a scouting party, while proceeding
along the west shore of the lake . _>ster
day, encountered a force of rebels strong

Cheatham reports that he drove t.:e
rebels from their position and that in the
engagement three Americans were kl'.leu
and two were wounded.
Rumors are In circulation In Mffnilathat
Major Cheatham discovered three Ameri
can prisoners who had been bound,
gagged and 3hot by the insurgents. rne:e
reports have not been confirmed, how
ever.
One Sandago, a lieutenant of the native
police, has been arrested and lodged in
jail, his subordinate having reported to
the authorities that he was em.eavoring
to enlist them In a plot to turn the police
against the Americans In the event of an
uprising.
Reports having reached the provost
marshal that arms were concealed at the
headquarters of the Dominican Friars, a
large building adjoining the place, a de
tachment of soldiers made a search of
the building. They found a small stock
of rifles, revolvers and ammunition, which
were confiscated despite the protests of
the Friars that the arms were not intend
ed for unlawful use. _n

YAQUIS READY
FOR A BATTLE
Forces Mobilizing at
Sahuripa.
m .
MEXICAN ARMY OFFICERS ARE
GREATLY ALARMED.
â¢
Had Prepared a Grand Coup to Ex
terminate the Indians, but They
Have Been Effectually
Checkmated.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
AUSTIN, Tex., Oct. -.-Information
reaching here to-night from Ortiz. State
of Sonora, Mexico, contains the following
information with reference to the Yaqui
Indian war:
Military scouting parties reaching the
military mobilization point at Torin, this
State, to-day, report that large forces of
Yaqui Indians are being mobilized at the
base of the mountains to the north of
that place, near Sahuripa. It is esti
mated that there are fully 200 Indians
there at present, and additional arrivals
are coming: in all the time. At the pres
ent time the Mexican forces, numbering
some 6000 troops in all. are mobilized in
or near Torin, and it is understood tne
object of their concentration at that point
ls to move into the Yaqui country at an
early date, probably about the middle of
next week, and open up a vigorous war
fare on the Indians, with a view of driv
ing them out and ending the war at once.
This mobilization of Mexican troops has
been conducted as quietly as possible,
with a view of sweeping down on the In
dians while they are still scattered
through the Yaqui country in small
bands, taking them by surprise and
sweeping them off the face of the earth,
orders having been given to take no pris
oners. The scouting parties, however,
state that the Indians have evidently
been informed of the mobilization of
troops, as they are mobilizing their forces
also and are preparing to offer a for
midable resistance in the advancement of
troops.
At present with 2500 men at their dis
posal at Sahuripa, which is considered
the key to the Yaqui country, the In
dians may be able to hold their own
against the troops with even half-way
fortifications. This reported mobilization
of the Indians was received with some
surprise by the Mexican forces, and may
possibly force them to change their plan
of action, but from reliable sources it is
learned that every effort will be made
to push the fight next week In the hope
of bringing it to a speedy termination.

SYSTEM RADICALLY WRONG

S;xÂ»c!ql Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Oct 13.-The annual
report of Major General Wesley Merritt.
commanding the' Department of the East,
headquarters at Governor's Island, New
York, was made public at the.U nited
States War Department to-day. The re
port is a strong arraignment of the whole
situation in the artillery branch of the
service, being particularly directed
against the, ordnance bureau and inci
dentally the quartermaster's department,
the latter for failure to furnish what
General Merritt considered proper quar
ters at artillery posts.
General Merritt says the system on
which' the artillery is organized Is wrong
in that the artilleryman, who is held re
sponsible for the work he has to do with
his guns, has no voice in the making or
selection of them, this being left entirely
to the ordnance bureau. But he adds that
Bince the engineer department in the
same â¢ independent way furnishes the
artilleryman with his emplacements and
there has been no fault to find with this
portion of the equipment; there must be
something more than the mere system to
blame for the unsatisfactory condition of
affairs. -: . . : :'â¢.''â '.
â¢ Jen-Till Merritt says he resumed com
ma no of that department on January 1
lust, since which time most of the in
fantry and cavalry in the department
have been ordered bo foreign .service,
leaving at present only four troops of
cavalry and three companies of infantry,
He- says he has no specific recommenda
tions to make regarding any one place.
The .[officers a,re known to be Intelligent,
industrious and serious in their efforts
to improve the efficiency of their com
mands, : â¢ '_;'â¢
"That the. coast artillery is not to-day
In -ah efficient condition," General Mer
ritt says, "is due to causes over which
artillery officers have no control; nor can
these impediments be reached by the de
partment commanders. Some require the
action of the Secretary of War and oth
ers can be remedied only by Congress.
The needs of the artillery are most ably
presented in the report of the department
artillery .inspector, to which I invite at
tention. i do not feci called upon to urge
th. specific remedies which he proposes;
but I certainly coincide with the opinion
he expresses that the artillery is in need
of remedial legislation. 1 deem it my
duty to direct attention to the defects on
Which Major Storey treats in detail and
then leave the quesion of how they shall
be removed to the law-making authorities
and their advisors.
"Our cavalry and infantry to-day are
organized on a modern basis, are splen
didly officered and equipped and with ' reg
ular supplies,' of food.. clothing and ammu
nition ..â '.main within themselves the ele
ments of complete efficiency. This is not
true of the artillery. The officers are well
educated md zealous, but their efforts
are crumped by Imperfect organization,
and responsibility . for the efficiency or
inefficieey. of their arm li. not as it
should, with themselves, but very largely
ln other quarters. Lather through defects
of law or their own inefficiency the sup
ply departments upon which the artillery
Ie wholly dependent has frequently failed
to loyally st rye its interests. The tact
that the engineer department, working
under the same law. has under its pres
ent chief given the artillery no cause for
complaint would seem to indicate' that
something is wrong besides the law.
"This condition gives rise to the oft
expressed desire of the' artillery "for a
chief of that arm of the service, who
would see that ii is supplied with what it
needs to be effective, but 1. personally
have grave doubts as to this being the
most suitable remedy, as it may result in
adding another bureau to the War De
partment, which may be as difficult to
make i ffli
'1 desire, li addition, to call special at
tention to th- following points in the re
port of the tiller. inspector:
"1. The failure of the quartermaster's
department to niak.- timely provision for
housing tbe garrisons at new artillery
posts.
"2. The necessity for providing at artil
lery posts a permanent post non-com
missioned staff of specialists, electrician
and observer sergeants.
"3. The recommendation that the
strength of the coast artillery be fixed at
the Dumber uf men and officers required
to fully mat! the suns.
"It is observe that the artillery should
he inspected by 'In- artillery officers and
11..;- fact that the department command
er --â¢ artillery inspector has no power to
inspect is a patent absurdity and one
which I urge may be disposed of at an
early date. The means of making any
arm of th" service all it should be should
rest with the peneral commanding the
geographical department In which the
eaid arm of the service is stationed "

OLD MINER RESCUED
BY THE DE VRIES PARTY
Captain Beck Found Unconscfipus
From Cold at Basalt
Meadows.
STOCKTON, Oct. Congressman May
rion de Vries and party, which left here 1
a week ago with the intention of making J
an extensive trip through the yosemite
Valley, has returned. De Vrles says win- \
ter has already come on with unusual
severity, and a trip over the trails was
impossible. Snow has already fallen as
far down as Fresno Flats, a condition not ;
known for years. All the Indians are
collecting and coming down from the]
higher altitudes. i The only, way to get in
now ls from the Inyo side, and if the'
I id weather continues this will be closed
before many days.
While at Basalt Meadows De Vries and
hi? companions picked up Captain Beck,
a well known mining man. The old man
had been In the higher altitudes on a j
prospecting trip. He started to come down
when the storm overtook him. Had he |
not struck the camp of the Congressman !
at Basalt Meadows he would surely have j
perished. When first discovered he was
In an unconscious condition. All the re- '
sources at hand were supplied him, but |
he- mained In a stupor and would not ,
awake from it. The question then was to |
get him out. Captain Beck was lashed j
to one of the horses with ropes and the :
way was made out slowly. It was still
bitterly cold, and Captain Beck did not |
revive during the whole trip. At last j
Fresno Plats was reached again, and !
there the old man was ensconced in the j
hotel and given all possible care. Just j
before the Stockton party left he had re- |
gained consciousness and seemed on a i
fair road to recovery. ;

the city to the amount of (4,700.000 for the
purchase of the plant of the Denver
Union Water Company, or the construc
tion of a new one. The other is to com
pel the street, railway company to heat Its
cars in winter. The Denver water Com
pany demands (9.000, 00 for Its plant.
MARIN LAWS BAR ANGLERS
The large body of anglers from this city
who have fished annually for steelheads
at the White House, Point Reyes, and at
Throckmorton Lagoon cannot do so this
winter. The Marin County officials passed
an ordinance in June last which prohibits
the taking of steelheads in any manner in
the streams under their jurisdiction. The
section of the ordinance covering this
matter reads as follows:
Any person who, In the County of Marin,
State of California, between the fifteenth
day of Octeber and the first day of April
in the following year shall catch, kill or de-
stroy any "salmon trout. ' "mountain
trout," "brook trout," "rainbow trout."
"speckled trout," or any other variety of
trout. Any person who shall, at any time,
catch, kill or destroy, with an; seine or net
or any spear, weir fence, banket, trap, grill
net, set net, dynamite or any poisonous,
deleterious or stupefying drug or other sub
stance or other Implement or appliance, any
trout named in this ordinance, or any spe
cies of trout in any of the streams, lakes or
waters lying within the count of Marin,
State of California, shall be guilty of a mis
demeanor. Provided, however, that any
person or persons may anide or catch trout
with hook and line between the ilrst day of
April and the fifteenth day of October ii.
each year.
John Butler has returned from Eel
River after a month's stay there. He
ha - nothing but good words for Humboldt
County and for its people. He was not
there for the best of the fishing, but nev
ertheless enjoyed good sport, On the day
before leaving for home he caught sev
enty-three half-pound steelheads.
Frank Maskey and wife and Fred Lees
and wife are In Humboldt County, divid
ing their time between duck and quail !
shooting and; the fishing.
G. W. Emmons. W. F. Bogart and Mr

urday and Sunday with moderate success.

LOMO EARNED
THE VERDICT
BY A HEAD
Beat P. A. Finnegan at Odds
of 25 to \ â Muddy Going
at Oakland,
It is an accepted thing on the turf that
long shots will bob up in rainy weather,
and as the track at Oakland yesterday
was a sea of dark, splashy mud, the un
expected was looked for in any of the
races. It finally occurred in the third, a
mile selling affair, when Lomo. a 25 to 1
shot, with Buchanan in the saddle,
snatched the verdict from P. A. Finnegan,
the second choice, by a head. Unfortu
nately the winner was almost entirely
overlooked. The supposed good thing In
the betting, Tennessee Maid, backed from
SO to 10, cut a very prominent figure out in
front until headed for the wire, when she
stopped. Roadrunner, after running in
second position for two-thirds of the
route, was downed for the place by Finne
gan. The latter horse was plainly the
best, but a bungling ride deprived him of
the honors.
In the other events decided favorites
captured two and second choices two of
the purses. The attendance was good and
the betting better than on the day pre
vious.
Burns & Waterhouse sent Essence, a
half-sister to Heartsease and Normandio,
to the post in the opening scramble for
maiden two-year-olds. The filly was
played down in the ring from 8 to 5 to
even money and won all the way. Moun
taineer, the mount of Bozeman, second
throughout the running, led Miss Vera out
two lengths for the place.
Trainer "Tommy" Lottridge and his
partner, "Jack" Atkin, missed winning a
four-handed jackpot with the long
striding Faversham in the mile run fol
lowing. The select coin was sent in on
Lothian, making that horse an even
money choice. Somis and Correct were
in slight demand. There was apparently
plenty of bottom to the course, and the
mud dressing distressed Faversham not
the least. Ruiz had the. black horse un
der wraps from the start, and he won
snickering from the favorite. The latter
was fortunate indeed to get the place,
for had Fauntleroy on Correct worn his
mud lamps the latter would have been
second. , : Â
Dr. Rowell's colors were seen In the
front for the first time at the meeting In
the seven furlong run, with but four
horses starting. Imperious, a 4 to 5 fa
vorite, with Jones up, spreadeagled his
company, winning ease,; up from Sor
row. Reolia, second choice, was away
from the barrier poorly, never being
able to get near the front.
Nine sprinters started in the final
five furlong dash, with Ftta II opening
a pronounced 6 to 5 favorite. The odds
against the chestnut mare receded to 5 to
2 Lily, and many no doubt were in
clined to think there was "nothing do
ing." Duffy had the leg up. and, trail
ins in fourth position to the stret<-b,
there made his move, and at the wire
downed Bamboulia, the post favorite,
without an effort. Campus took the
show from February.

OLYMPIC PUGILIST
BEAT PHILADELPHIAN
OAKLAND. Oct. 13.-Pete Raconi of
the Olympic Club took exactly three min
utes to-night to defeat Billy Springfield
of Philadelphia at the Reliance Club. The
gong sounded just as Springfield was de
clared out. The round was perhaps the
liveliest ever seen at the Reliance. Both
men were in good condition, and have
records for science. One minute was spent
In walking around each other, and then
the work began. Some hard blows were
struck by both men. when suddenly Ra
coni turned himself loose. He flew' after '
his man. and before Springfield realized ;
the rapidity with which Raconi was fight- I
ing the work was done. Springfield went <
down once, but only remained about six ;
seconds. There was a repetition' of light- j
ning fighting, and just as Springfield was
counted out the gong rang and the de- â¢
feated man was assisted to his comer.
Jack McMahon and W. Mever, both
from San Francisco, were matched for
ten rounds, but in the sixth Meyer was
knocked out. He was outclassed from the ;
start.
Charles Viekers and Mike Moliss went |
ten rounds, and though both knew very
little about boxing they put up a very ac- ]
ceptable hippodroming act and furnished
good entertainment to the crowd. Some
of the rounds were so exciting that they
brought the audience to its feet- veiling
applause to the tired hut game fighters.
Viekers earned the decision.
The programme was one of the best
seen at a local club for many months.
KENNEDY AND CHILDS DRAW.
Six Hot Rounds Fought Before a
Chicago Athletic CluB!
CHICAGO, Oct.. 13.â Kennedy of
California and Frank Childs of Chicago
fought six hot rounds to-night, the de
cision being a draw. Kennedy, who out
weighed Childs. had the better of his an
tagonist a majority of- the rounds, but
Childs did most of the leading through
out. Kennedy had the better of It the
third round, his left ja_s leaving Childs
somewhat unsteady. Kennedy put it all
over Childs in the first half of the fourth
round, landing left jabs and right swings
until Childs was weak. The latter came
up strong at the finish of the round, how
ever, and Kennedy was himself a little
unsteady when the bell rang. The decision
was well received.

FAIR GOLFERS COMPETE.
Mrs. Fox and Miss Underhill to Meet
in the Finals.
PHILADELPHIA, Oct. 13.-Mrs. Caleb
Fox of the Huntingdon Valley Country
Club of Rydale, Pa., and Miss Ruth Un
derbill of the Nassau Golf Club of Glen
cove, L. 1.. won their matches in the semi
final round for the woman's golf cham
pionship of the United States to-day.
These players will play off the final round
to-morrow for the championship.
The semi-final round of the consolation
class for the trophy offered by the
Women's Golf Association of Philadelphia
was also played to-day. In this round
Miss Terry of Irvington, N. V., beat Mrs.
W. F. Morgan of Short Hills, N. J., two
up. and Miss Lucy Herron of Cincinnati
beat Miss Katherine M. Rowland of
Greenwich. Conn., four up. three to play.
The winners play for the trophy to-mor
row.
liaising Sugar Beets.
MONTEREY, Oct. 13.â The ranchers of
Carmel Valley, between this place and Sa
linas, began raising sugar beets for the
first time this year, and their experiment
has been so successful that many of them
intend going into beet culture on a
large scale. During the past week several
carloads of beets have been shipped from
the valley to the Spreckels sugar factory
at Salinas.
WHITE WINGS
WILL SOON BE
SNUGLY FOLDED
Closing Days of the Yachting
Season Are Close at
Hand.
The Victory of the Gadder has produced
one excellent result for the San Francisco
Yacht Club, no fewer than twenty-one
persons, four or five of whom are ladies,
having handed in their names for mem
bership since September 23. Though th*
Corinthians were naturally disappointed
at the defeat of the Truant they bore the
misfortune like true sportsmen. The
change of possession of the Challenge cup
will be a good tiling for yachting and will
aid materially in building up the San Fran
cisco Club. It will also add greatly to the
Interest' of the yachting season of 1900,
for the Corinthians will try their hardest
to recover the trophy, and the Gadder will
make a strong bid for next year's Mac
donough cup.
Last Sunday evening there was a warm
north wind j which induced most of the
yachtsmen at Sausalito to breakfast
ashore. At Ba. m. the clubhouse gun was
fired, and. the officers' flags and burgees
were run up to the masthead. Several of
the Corinthian and California yachtsmen
who had come over for the presentation
of the cup on the previous night paid calls
on Commodore T. 1.. Mill on board the flag
ship gnus. Toward 11 o'clock the fleet
got under way at a signal from Commo
dore T. L. Hill. Commodore Allen M.
Clay saluted and bade his hosts farewell,
then set his course for Oakland Creek.
Off Goat Island the breeze fell light, but
the California yachts Pactolus, Embla,
Idler and Surf after some delay made
their moorings. The San Francisco and
Corinthian yachts moved up to Paradise
Cove and dropped anchor there. The
schooners Ramona, Chispa and Lurline,
the yawls :Tramontana and Arcturus and
the sloops Cygnus, Aeolus. Thelma, Phoe
nicia, Freda, Nixie and Presto were under
way last Sunday.
G. T. S White, ex-vice commodore of
the San Francisco Yacht Club, has sold
the sloop Will o' the Wisp, with all -her
equipment, and for the first time In sev
eral years owns no boat of any sort.
This afternoon the Tiburon fleet will
cruise to Corinthian Cove, and to-night a
chowder supper, prepared under the direc
tion of Port Captain John H. Keefe, will
be served on the beach. To-morrow the
small-yacht regatta and rowooat races
postponed from September 17 are sched
uled to take place. As the yawl Kitti
wake and the sloop Doris are the only
half-raters available the small-yacht re
gatta will probably fall through. There
may, however, be some rowing races, and
there will certainly be a baseball game
between th" "Keegans" and the "Fan
cies," the former captained by "Keegan"
Schober and the latter by "Fancy"
Thornton. Of the last four annual base
ball games each .nine has won two. The
Corinthians will give a closing jinks next
Saturday, the. 21st Inst., at the clubhouse,
but the reception which has in some for
mer years bet given in the afternoon will
be abandoned. The Encinals and San
Franciscos will close on the same day.
On Sunday, the 22d. the last cruises in
squadron will lie taKen.
The handsome schooner yacht Jessie,
formerly owned by J. M. Macdonough and
Bold some time ago to be used as a pilot
boat on the Columbia River, is. to be em
ployed in halibut fishing on the banks off
Cape Flattery and Cape Fox.
The South Bay Club has postponed its
regatta till the. spring of 1900 on account
of the prevailing light winds. The yachts
men around Alviso are having great sport
spearing the leopard sharks which come
up to. the. shore after the crabs. A. C.
Eaton of the sloop Feu Collet recently
speared several which were about four
feet long and weighed fifty pounds each.
The sharks destroy many small fish.
Commodore 11. A. Spencer's schooner
Muriel is being repainted.

last race was a severe test on the birds,
owing to the extreme heat. The represen

BASS FOR EEL RIVER.
Fish Commissioners to Stock the
Waters of Humboldt County.
Deputies Fish Commissioners Davis and
Cross were in San Pablo Bay yesterday
seining for small striped bass, which the
Commissioners are to send to Bel River,
Humboldt County, on next Monday. It is
the desire to plant at least .no fry in that
stream. The first lot sent was not a cus
cess; all but a dozen dying en route. The
Commissioners, have been puzzled over
the fact that the -fish have not sought
those waters of their own accord. Soon
after these fish became plentiful in the
waters, of San Francisco Bay the Com
missioners found that the overflow had
migrated. to the south until now they are
plentiful in all of the southern streams
having a' permanent opening into the
ocean. ..",..-. .'â¢* '"-â¢â¢

TO LEAVE THE
REGULAR RANK.
He Will Be Held as a 1
Volunteer.

THOSE WHO WILL BE AIDS
â¢
RAIN MAKES THE CAMPS UN
COMFORTABLE.
IThe Luckless Thirty-First Will Be
Released From Quarantine, but
Not From Its Island
Isolation.
Although General Shafter has not re
ceived official information of the fact,
still there is every reason to believe he
will remain in the service as a volunteer
officer, or an officer of volunteers, after
his retirement as a brigadier general in
the regular army. Monday next he must
retire, but before that time he will hear
from Washington regarding any service
to follow.
Dispatches from the East carry the in
formation that the President intends to
retire him finally as a major general of
regulars, or even a lieutenant general;
but such a thing Is impossible. He must
retire from the regular army on Mon
day, and he cannot be appointed to any
higher rank than that of brigadier, as
there is no vacancy above him, and there
will be no vacancy by the time of his
retirement. After he has been retired he
cannot be appointed to any position
higher or lowerâ in the regular army. It
may be possible, however, to advance him
to be a lieutenant general of volunteers,
but such a course has no precedent and
is not likely to be adopt" d.
If General Shafter remains in the ser
vice after Monday it will bring good for
tune to Captaitt J. D. Leitch, recently
promoted into the Twenty-fifth Infantry,
lie is spoken of as one of | !<..â¢ aids to
General Shafter. and In that case Cap
tain Leitch will be retained on this coast
and in this department. Captain Kit
tredge. General Shaffer's son-in-law. will
be the second aid. and First Lieutenant
Wilson of the Third Artillery is to re
main in his present position.
Captain Kittredge was a captain and
assistant adjutant general on General
Shafter"s staff during the Cuban cam
paign, but soon after the return of the
general i.i was mustered out. Since the
recruiting of the new regiments he has
been mentioned fo,r a commission as .â .
line officer, and a few days ago he was
recommissioned, this time as a captain
ln the Forty-sixth Infantry. He was Im
mediately mad.- an aid to General Shaf
ter. Should General Shafter remain Cap
tain Kittredge will have a much sought
for position, but if the general be retired
as well from the volunteers as from the
regulars Captain Kittredge will have to
join his regiment.
The plan of recruiting a battalk n of the
Fortieth and one of the Forty-third at
the Presidio has been abandoned and the
present intention is to recruit but two
companies of each. A few days ago eight
of the officers destined for the battalion
of the Fortieth being formed here were
ordered East to Fort Riley, where the
regime .(quarters have been estab
lished, and ere were left only sufficient
officers for two companies with Major
Case, who will command the battalion, in
charge. 8
The rain made the volunteer ran at
the Presidio particularly uncomfortable,
but the returning volunteers have had
all kinds of tropical rain poured down
upon them and they do not mind the
showers that fall here. They had to
stand the Islam! weather without the
shelter of tents, too. so they suffered but
little at home. The volunteers on their
way to the islands had not so much ex
perience, however, and except those who
saw service in Cuba they had a bail time
of it. The surgeons' reports from all the
exposed commands show no evil effects
however, be the rain on the whole has
done the men no harm. It has left mud
and to spar* all around the post, but that
is about air it has done.
The two cases of sickness, supposed to
be diphtheria, sent to the genera hos
pital from the Twenty-eighth Regiment
have turned out to be only severe cases
of tonsilltis. No others have been re
ported.
To-day the Thirty-first Regiment will
emerge from quarantine, but that will
bring small consolation to the men of the
command. The regiment will not be
taken from Angel Island, but it will wait
there for transportation to Manila.
There will be a transport assigned to the
command as soon as possible, but it may
be some days before the boys will get
away, and in the meantime they will re
main in isolation unless Colonel Pettlt
Ie generous In the way of passes.
John A. Logan, private In Troop I. of

eral hospital last Wednesday, was buried
in the National Cemetery yesterday with
military honors. ,'
The Montana and Kansas men will
spend to-day in Oakland as the guests of
the city. They will form at the camps
fit the Presidio at half-past 7 o'clock and
will march to the ferry in a body.
Leave of absence for ten days has been
granted to Second Lieutenant John B.
Caldwell of the Washington Regiment.

PERFECTED THE PLANS
FOR THE GOLDEN JUBILEE
M. T. Moses, Exercises.
Louis Kragen, Information.
Chairmen of the Odd Fellows' Grand Fete.
THE group of portraits presented is made of six of the chairmen of sub-committees of the executive committee of the
Golden Jubilee of Odd Fellowshio in California. During the past six months they have been engaged in arranging
the details of the work that each committee has had assigned to it, and now they have perfected their plans so that
when the celebration starts in the early part of next week all will be in readiness.
The executive committee at its meeting Saturday night spent several hours in arranging the little details to make
the several features of the celebration a success.
Reports v ere received from several portions of the State which assure a large attendance from the interior More
than 300 will come from Sacramento, and will be accompanied by a baud.
ELLERT OBJECTED TO
FOREIGN SCAVENGERS
'TIS SAID THAT'S WHY HE
RESIGNED HIS POSITION.
Others Say the Italians Objected to
Eliert, but the Ex-Mayor Has
the Papers.
The recently announced resignation of ;
ex-Mayor L. K. Eliert from the manage- j
ment of the Sanitary Reduction Company
has created more discussion "on the j
street" and in political circles than any j
recent occurrence in finance. A. dozen ru- j
mors are in the air as to his reason for
stepping out, and all of them put the
Italian members of the company and their
objection to Ellert's administration be- I
hind It.
That a crisis in the affairs of the as- ,
sociation was approaching has been ap- ;
parent for the past six months. During
that time Mr. Ellert's greatest endeav
ors have been directed to keeping peace
with the Italian scavengers and their fel
low-countrymen and sympathizers among
the stockholders who assumed Ellert's ;
policy as personal slight upon them- ;
selves. , .
Knowing this, the wise ones along the I
street were prone to believe the rumor j
that the Italian members had finally got j
the upper hand and demanded the resigna
tion of the objectionable manager. How ;
they got control of the company stock
was a question no one could answer, but
It was generally said that A. Sbarboro, :
the secretary, had been gradually buying
up holdings here and there, until he had
secured enough to swing things his way.
Sbarboro, It was Bald, was acting as i
agent for the Italian element. Mr. Eliert j
yesterday denied that any of these theo
ries were true and also that his resigna
tion was anything but voluntary. In, sup
port of his statement he displayed a con
tract with the company which did not
expire until 1903. "The fact of the matter
is," said he, "that I have resigned solely
for the reason that my work was becom
ing decidedly distasteful to me. The last ,
few months it has got so that all my ;
time has been taken up in petty fights I
and the settlement of small differences
with a lot of scavengers, and I got sick
Of it. My contract with the company did
not expire until 1903. bo that the story
about an enforced resignation is palpably
untrue. I am still the largest stockholder
In the company, and my interest in Its \
business will be as great as heretofore. j
I have simply unshouldered responsi- 'â 
bility."
TO SHOOT OVER SUTRO'S.
Work Begins on Fortifications Back
of the City Cemetery.
Next week the Government's construct
ors, of fortifications will begin work on i
one of the largest pieces of defensive en
gineering to be placed at any port on the I
coast. The project has long been under
consideration and now the plans are j all
prepared for the "workmen to begin. ' It
is the construction of huge works of
masonry, '-earth, sand and rock for the In
stallment of some of the largest g<jns
that, have ever come to this State on the
high hills between Sutro's and the old
City Cemetery. The fortifications when
complete will be the best at this port,
as the guns will command a full sweep
of the ocean for miles along the coast.
They would be particularly destructive
to a hostile fleet endeavoring to enter the
bay.
About the time of the war with Spain,
it was proposed to rush the work, as the*
Dons had a considerable fleet on the Wa
ters of the Pacific. On May 1 a year ago
Dewey nut the ships of Spain under
water, where they would he safe so far
as this coast was concerned and tho
Government had the work stopped as un
necessary at the time. However, it was I
not dropped and, as stated, work is to be '
resumed next week. All of the lorce of j
workmen, which Is Indeed large, has been
engaged and will begin on Monday.- It is
calculated that it will take at least three |
years to get the fortifications ready for
the Installment of the big guns.

MITCHELL FIRED IN
HIS OWN DEFENSE
VERDICT IN THE McAUEIFFE
INQUEST.
Captain Gilien Testifies Strongly in
Favor of the Prisoner That He
Was Bleeding From ooe's
Fist.
The McAuliffe inquest was concluded
yesterday with the testimony of Police
Captain Gilien and at its conclusion the
jury returned a verdict to the effect that
the deceased, Frank McAuliffe, met his
death from the effects of a gunshot
wound inflicted by Frank Mitchell in self
defense and that the homicide was justi
fiable.
Captain Gillen's testimony was favora
ble to the prisoner. At the time of his ar
rest Mitchell was bleeding at the nose and
mouth and was considerably under, the
Influence of liquor. He told the witness
that while he and the two McAuliffes
were drinking together in the saloon he
remarked to Joe McAuliffe, "Joe. you are
now in a good position and able to pay
me that little bill." McAuliffe asked how
large the bill was and upon Mitchell re
plying called Mitchell an opprobrious
name and said that he would never pay
him. McAuliffe followed up the Insult
ing language with a blow in the mouth,
knocking Mitchell down.. Mitchell went
back to 'lis- grocery and got his revolver
and returned to the place where the Mc-
Auliffes were. The dispute recommenced
and when both brothers were- advancing
upon Mitchell with the apparent purpose
of attacking him he tired and fdiot Frank
McAuliffe. : ; ;
