DESERTER LELAND HAS
A TALE OF ADVENTURE
TOOK MALARIA ON THE EVE
OF A BATTLE.
Captured by the Confederates, Es
caped and Assumed the Name
of a Dead Comrade for a
Pension.
William Leland, the ex-soldier arrested
last Thursday at the Soldiers' Home at
Yountville, Napa County, by United
States Marshal Shine on a warrant from
Augusta, Me., charging him with having
falsely sworn to a pension claim, was
taken. before United States Commissioner
Heacock yesterday and ordered sent to
Maine for trial.
Leland is past 60 years of age, and tells
an interesting story. He freely admits I
his guilt, but urges in his defense that he ;
was sorely tempted by reason of his pov
erty and sickness to provide for himself I
by means of a pension to which he was j
not entitled, and that he yielded to the |
temptation. He says that he enlisted in '<
the Thirteenth New York Regiment at
the breaking out of the Civil War and I
served under Burnside at the time of the !
first battle of Fredericksburg. On the :
night of that engagement he was at
tacked with malaria, and while under the
influence of a high fever he stole away
from camp to get a drink of water. He :
was captured by a Confederate outpost i
and placed in the hospital at Danville i
and made his escape eight months after- !
ward. He then went into West Virginia
and surrendered to the first Union pro
vost marshal that he found. There he
discovered that his regiment had been
mustered out and that he had been listed
as a deserter, so that he would be de
barred from going into a soldiers' home or
getting a pension.
He went to Chelsea, Me., about two
years ago and took the name of a com
rade of his company in the Thirteenth
Regiment. This comrade and his wife
had died at Rochester, X. V"., and Leland
thought that if he received a pension in
the name of the dead man no one would
be Injured thereby and that the Govern
ment was rich enough to stand it. Two
acquaintances who had known him only
under the assumed name swore to his
application with him and it .was sent to
the Pension Commissioners, who discov
ered the fraud.
Edmond Godchaux Resigns
Chief Deputy Assessor Edmond God
chaux resigned from his position yester
day afternoon. Assessor Dodge accepted
the resignation, but stated that he will
not fill the vacancy. Mr. .Godchaux has
been nominated for Recorder on the Dem
ocratic ticket, and decided that he could
not fill his official duties and manage his
campaign at the same time.
FOLLOWERS OF
EASTERN STAR
TO MEET HERE
Opening of Communi
cation Tuesday.
GRAND CHAPTER TO CONVENE
FACTS ABOUT THE ORIGIN OF
THE GREAT ORDER.
Laboring for the Progress, Pros
perity and Perfection of the
Teachings of Free
Masonry.
The twenty-seventh annual communi
cation of the Grand Chapter of Califor
nia and Nevada of the Order of the East
ern Star will be holden in the Sherman-
Clay Hall in this city commencing at 1
o'clock next Tuesday afternoon. The
present grand officers are: Worthy grand
patron, Toseph Martin Lawrence, M. D.,
Los Angeles; worthy grand matron. Mrs.
Maria Alters Pierce, Oakland; associate
grand patron, George Louis Darling, San
Francisco; associate grand matron, Mrs.
Marie Louise Coffin. Carson , City, Nov.:
grand secretary, Mrs. Kate Josephine
Willats, San Francisco; grand treasurer,
Mrs. Kate Stephens Craig, Lake County;
grand conductress, Mrs. Carrie Lois An
derson; associate grand conductress, Mrs.
Belle Williams; grand chaplain, Mrs.
Mary Stewart Smith; grand marshal,
Mrs. Lillian Warner; grand Adah, Mrs.
Mabel Blanchard Swisler; grand . Ruth,
Mrs. Harriet Freeman; grand Esther,
Mrs. Louise Plummer; grand Martha,
Mrs. Anna Burgess Miller; grand Electa,
Mrs. Kate Flint; grand organist, Mrs.
Rose Kahn; grand warder, Mrs. Mary
Jeanette Robinson: grand sentinel, Mrs.'
May Carpenter Ellis.
During the session the committee on
home, which was appointed in .889, will j
probably make its final report and be per- j
mitted to retire with honors well earned
and worthily bestowed, /he following
facts not generally known concerning the I
pioneer order for ladies in this State will j
appear in the report:
1 The ritualistic work of the Adoptive Rite of j
I Eastern Star, as it was originally styled, was
written by the founder. Robert Morris, in Feb
ruary, 1850. For several years, at private gath
-1 erings .of Masons and their female relatives, he ;
I communicated the Eastern Star degree to those j
' present and during his lectures before Masonic :
I lodges he spread the "glad tidings." Still his j
I efforts to systematize the work were very un
satisfactory to him, and it was not until Rob
ert Macoy. in 1868. proposed the plan of East- |
crn Star chapters, now in vogue, that Mr Mor- I
ris felt that at last his labor of love was :
crowned with success.
Alpha Chapter No. 1 of New Tork City was
the first one organized and instituted under the :
perfected Macoy system, and it was the direct j
result of a Masonic fair, which was opened on
, the sth of December, 1566, in the French church
j at Grand and Crosby streets, in aid of the Ma-
I sonic Hall and asylum fund. It lasted for
weeks, proved a great success and netted about
$60,000. The Masons fully appreciating the act-
I ive part the ladies had taken in the fair, and
anxious to keep alive their mutual interests,
proposed organizing a permanent society to be
composed of the. ladles who had assisted at
the fair, most of them having possession of
the Eastern Star degree. In view of this, J.
F. Waring, past master of Enterprise Lodge
No. 228, F. and A. M., at the request of many
members of the Masonic fraternity, on the 17th
of January, 1867, called for a meeting of the
ladies connected with the fair, in Odd Fellows'
building, on the 21st of the month, to organize
a society of the Sisters of the Eastern Star.
The society was organized, and from the date
of its organization the . members took a most
active part in all Masonic work, particularly
in reference to the hall and asylum fund. In
March following the organization the members
gave a reception in aid of the fund, which
netted a neat sum, and in order to make the
amount an even $1000 the society voted $27 out
of its treasury. For a time the members held
house to house meetings, but in time rented a
'hall at 694 Broadway, where, on the Sth of Oc
tober, 1868, Robert Macoy for the first time
used his revised ritual in manuscript form,
before a large audience composed of Masons
and Sisters of the Eastern Star, and exempli
fied what Is. now popularly known as the Order
of the Eastern Star. Soon after, December 28,
1868, Alpha Chapter No. 1 was instituted and
the officers Installed by Macoy. grand patron.
Then followed Friendship Family No. 103 of
Brooklyn, chartered by Morris In IS6fi, which
received a charter from Macoy" ln 1569. and took
the name of Esther Chapter No. 2. Since then
the order has spread in every direction, until
now there are thirty grand chapters In as many
States of the Union. Golden Gate Chapter No.
1 of California was chartered under the Macoy
system on the 10th of May. 1569, and the order
in this State has been in a most flourishing
condition for more than thirty years. Its mem
bership at this time ls about 11.000.
â As a conclusion "H. A. H." has the fol
lowing: "The 'Sisters of the Eastern
Star' who stand to-day in their pride and
strength should pause 'lest they forget*
that they are indebted to a Master Ma

finally that the whole of the Eastern Star

the order connot itself exist without the
support of the Masonic fraternity. Al
though the 'sisters' owe a debt cf grati
tude which they can never cancel and
stand alone, it is still their privilege to
show that they, are in earnest when they
ask to labor at the same shrine. 'Look

the mission of the 'Sisters of the Eastern
Star' to make the Masonic Widows' and

and the grandest home in the United

live and labor for the progress, prosperity

SAN FRANCISCO'S OPPORTUNITY.
REPORTS from Chicago announce that Marcus
A. Hanna intends to issue a call for the Re
publican National Committee to meet in Wash
ington City early in December, and that it is believed
the committee will then select the place at which fo
hold .the Presidential convention next year. To the
report is added the statement that the cities most
frequently spoken of as suitable places for the con
vention are St. Louis, Cincinnati, Cleveland, Mil
waukee/Minneapolis and San Francisco.
It will be seen that Eastern people have begun
to consider San Francisco's fitness as a meeting
place for national conventions without any particular
urging on our part. California is no longer regarded
as a remote section of the Union too distant to be
conveniently visited. The improvement of railway
facilities and telegraph communication, the success
of the numerous national' gatherings of various as
sociations held here, and last, but not least, the im
pression made upon the country at large by the man
ner in which the city has received the volunteers
both when going to the Philippines and when re
turning home, have had an educational effect upon
the Eastern mind. San Francisco from this time
forth will be looked upon always as one of the great
convention cities of the nation and its claims will be
carefully considered by those who are to decide
where conventions are to be held.
These facts give encouragement to the movement
now started to bring to San Francisco at least one,
and possibly both, of the national Presidential con
ventions of next year. There was never before so
good an opportunity for carrying such a movement to
success as at this time. It is not likely there will be
much of a contest over the nominations in either
party, and consequently the advocates of different
leaders will not have to consider the advantage of
holding the convention in a city favorable to their
candidate. The personal factor is eliminated, and a
city whose population is impartial, like San Fran
cisco, has a better chance to win than it would have
were there any question of individual favoritism at
issue.
The many advantages of this city as a place for
summer conventions are well known to our own peo
ple and are beginning to be understood elsewhere.
In no other large city in the United States is there
such pleasant and healthful weather in the summer
as that which prevails here. Delegates to a conven
tion to San Francisco in June need have no fear of
prostration from heat, or sunstrokes, or other ill
effects of the heated season in the East.
Furthermore, our hotel accommodation has now
been amply proven to be sufficient to meet all the
demands that will be made upon it by the largest con
vention that is likely to assemble. The trip across
the continent is now a pleasure tour rather than a
fatiguing journey, and can be undertaken without
serious inconvenience to any one.
All of these things it wall be easy to impress upon
the national committees of the two great parties if
we set about it rightly. That is now the issue before
our citizens. It is one that must be attended to
without delay. Statements made in interviews with
representatives of The Call and published yesterday
and this morning show that strong, and influential
men are willing to support the movement with energy
and zeal. The prospect of success is therefore good.
Let all unite and make San Francisco the convention
city of the nation in 1900.
At this stage of the game it might be well to point
out to the Associated Press that what the country
wants from the Transvaal is news and not editorial
comment with a strong British bias. So far that
is about all it has been transmitting.
Edmond Godchaux, Democratic candidate for Re
corder, has jarred the belief of many of the faithful
by resigning as chief deputy to Assessor Dodge.
No Democrat was ever known to do anything of the
kind.
Francisco Morosco, a Sutter Creek laborer, had
a theory that he could smoke with safety in the im
mediate vicinity of blasting powder. He* tried it,
and it is' needless to say that the theory exploded.
â¦
If Germany, France, Russia or any other power
has a bone to pick with Great Britain now is the
time to do it.
IF THE RAIN WERE STORED!
THE rains have fallen timely and over much of
the ground in sufficient volume to enable the
plowing of ground, in preparation for seeding.
In the Eastern States, where the land is locked by
deep frost, the farmer waits for winter to break and
the ground to thaw, and then seedtime is upon him.
Here the farmer waits for summer to break and the
ground to be moistened by the early rains and then
goes afield with his plow.
The summer has broken ; the soil is moistened
and will soon be turned. Ahead is the prospect of a
rainy season of unusual length and fall. The rain on
the plains and foothills and the snow in the moun
tains will be abundant, and many will forget the
time of lack and the fallow fields and withered
orchards that it caused.
There are many, however, who, remembering the
drought and knowing the certainty that such years
will come again, will persist in measures that will
avert their consequences and make every year fruit
ful.
" To these men the promise of abundant rain in the
coming season is a promise of drought averted in the
future if the great fall of flood water could be stored
for use when needed. In the rains expected between
now and next May there will be waste. The rivers
will leave their banks, the levees around the garden
islands in the delta of the Sacramento and San Joa
quin will in many cases break and prop
erty will be destroyed. The waste waters, vagrant
and merciless, will wander at will and destroy as
they go. They will wash away the soil and carry it
into the rivers, filling their channels to the destruc
tion of navigation, and will inflict injury upon pro
duction and transportation, the two first in the State's
trinity of wealth-producing factors.
If the State were prepared now to hold the flood
waters in the foothills. in reservoirs at such an alti
tude that the loose soil is not carried with them and
from which their distribution may reach every part of
the valleys below, the rain now hailed with enthu
siasm would have a more than doubled significance.
Now it is received gratefully for what land it makes
fruitful, and with misgivings for what its floods may
destroy. Then it would be saluted as a blessing un
alloyed by loss. It would mean permanent provision
against drought; unimpaired and permanent navi
gability of our meandered rivers; a constant supply
for. mining every day in the year and every year; a
quadrupiication of our grain and fruit and forage
producing area; and such an assurance of the per
manence of agriculture and horticulture as would
add immensely to our population of that desirable
class of small land-holders who are the true expres
sion of the might and majesty of the State. With
such a reservoir system, assuring the permanency of
water and its use at a reasonable charge, a family can
prosper here on the product of twenty or forty acres,
an area within its own capacity to cultivate. There is
no other part of the Union, nor of the world, where
an acre of land will produce as much and in as great
variety as here, not excepting the valley of the Po,
provided the water supply is made permanent. This
is proved over and over again, in the San Joaquin
Valley, in places where the normal flow of the streams
suffices for such permanent supply.
The convention called in this city for the 14th of
next month may come here under umbrellas and in
gumboots. It may meet in the rain, and the windows
of heaven may be open throughout its sessions, but
nevertheless it must meet, and the ranchers, miners,
lumbermen, bankers, shippers and business men of
this State must so realize its importance as to make
it impressive. The rain that falls while it deliberates
means a fat year to come, but still, ahead, famine
smitten, dry and fruitless, lurks and waits a lean year
to devour its substance. That convention can make
California entirely safe from loss by drought or flood.
The engineering problem is simple, the construction
cheaper than anywhere else in the world, the line of
distribution shorter. The water that has been re
leased from one of these reservoirs under a head
and pressure that make electric power to run many
mines, passes from the turbine or Pelton wheel it
has actuated to make grain, grass and fruit grow
within a few miles of the spot where it generated
power to dig and mill gold. Why not harness all this
wasted power and fertility so that such a rainy season
as is coming will represent all gain and no loss?
If Bill Bryan is still out for the stuff, as he was
when he wrote that letter to J. Sterling Morton,
row's his chance to realize. Owners of Columbia
and Shamrock will undoubtedly compensate him
handsomely for the use of his bellows to fill the sails
of their boats.
CITY BOSSES.
THE intention of the new primary law was to end
the rule of bosses by getting together untram
, meled municipal conventions. The history of the'
recent Democratic convention shows that this inten
tion was in some way defeated. The convention was
neither a free agent nor an independent body. It was
bossed and dragooned as completely as any that ever
met in this city in the worst days of openly avpwed
boss rule.
So completely was it under the thumb of two
men, Mr. Pheian and McNab, that The Call was able
to publish its ticket before it met, and did publish it,
through the simple process of finding out who were
on the slate of the two men who did the bossing.
The convention itself might as well have met and
adjourned sine die and let Mr. Pheian and Mr. Mc-
Nab announce the ticket. It may be said that they
are new bosses, untried, and that the result of their
bossing may justify it. But the point is that if they
can secure power by such processes in their party
that will be proof that any other two men may do
the same, and by putting the government in their
hands the way is opened for any other two to do the
same, regardless of how they will use power when
they get it.
It is the first step that costs, and if this city is to
ratify and accept the methods of these it bends its
neck for the yoke of others using the same methods,
no matter how heavy it may be.' The methods which
shut out all advice, prevented all freedom, banished
all deliberation from the Democratic convention and
treated with contempt the friends and supporters of
men as worthy as any who were nominated by
Messrs. Pheian and McNab, are not methods to be
encouraged by the voters of a city like San Fran
cisco. Just such methods have proven ultimately to
be the means of bad government wherever they have
been adopted.
It is noteworthy that the henchmen, the drill
masters and file-closers chosen to swing the rawhide
and carry out the orders of the two bosses were the
trained and seasoned thralls of the several dy
nasties of Democratic bosses who have long
ruled the politics and manipulated the schemes
of that party in this city. They knew how
to carry out orders, how to suppress free expression,
how to walk rough shod over the right ambitions of
the worthy, and they did it all with the rough skill
of artists in their line, accustomed to working ' a
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, OCTOBER 14, 1899.
machine that treats principles as chaff and works
only for place and power for the few who operate it:
Mr. Pheian, in the exuberance of success, with the
confidence of a self-created candidate and absorbed
in admiration of his creator, smiled upon himself
while frowning upon others, and opened his cam
paign by an attack on his opponent which will be
resented by every self-respecting citizen. .
Republicans have before them a plain duty which
should be a pleasure. They will draw to them the in
dependent voters in support of Horace Davis and
the ticket he leads, and they will carry it to victory.
Ir their municipal convention men were not found in
a mad scramble for office; they were there deliber
ating, discussing and seeking candidates of spotless
private and public character to carry the standard of
genuine reform to victory. While they were doing
this wholesome duty Mr. Pheian was sitting on the
housetops proclaiming himself the civic savior of the
city, the sole incarnation of municipal reform and
the only man worthy to be trusted. There are scores of
delegates to the last Democratic State convention who
have not forgotten the incidents in the nomination of
a Justice of the Supreme Court, in which he made a
figure inconsistent with his uttered estimate of him
self.
He has twice succeeded in being elected Mayor by
Republican votes, through professions of non
partisanship, and now hopes for a third time to win
by the same means. He admits that when he called
Hon. Horace Davis "a traitor" it expressed his idea,
and it did. He regards every man, in his own party
and out, who stands in the way of his ambition, as a
traitor. ' In that mood he bossed his own conven
tion, and by easy transfer carries it over in an attack
upon his Republican opponent.
i It is time for the Republicans, who are the major
ity stockholders in this corporation, to resume charge
of its affairs, and they intend to do it.
P Lakes of Killarney are still unsold. Perhaps
if the country surrounding them were cut up into
town lots some enterprising American real estate
speculator, with an experience in suburban ventures,
might undertake to get rid of them. Nature's beauties
are not frequently placed on the auction block.
AMENDING THE WAR TAX LAW.
DISPATCHES from Washington confirm the
statements of The Call that Congress will be
called upon at the coming session to amend
, the war tax law for the purpose of eliminating cer
tain defects which have resulted in diminishing the
amount of revenue expected to be. derived from it.
In discussing the subject an agent in this city of
the Internal Revenue Department is quoted as' say
ing the greatest loss has been due to a decision of the
Attorney General that undivided profits in banks are
. not to be considered as capital or surplus and there
fore are not subject to the tax on undivided surplus.
j He estimates that in the northeastern districts of Cali
, fornia alone the Government loses between $200,000
and $300,000 a year by that ruling.
â  The agent is reported to have added: "Some
money is lost on the wine tax by restaurant keepers
serving wine in pitchers instead of bottles which are
taxed, and also by the use of tax stamps a second
: and a third time. This loss could be avoided by
taxing the wine in the winery, or by having strip
stamps fastened to the cork and to the bottle in such
I a way that the cork could not be drawn without
breaking the stamp. I have no doubt that Congress
will amend the law so that those leakages will be
checked."
The defects pointed out are but a few of the many
that have been noted in the law. They. serve, how
eevr, to show how necessary it is to revise the law
and materially amend it. The wine tax, for example,
; ought to be abolished utterly or so completely al
tered as to make it something quite different from
what it is. As it stands it is virtually a tax upon
California alone. There is no other State in the
Union where the people use light wines habitually
at. meals, and consequently a tax on wines making no
discrimination between the costliest brands and the
light wines used at the table is unfair. If the wine
tax be continued it should at least impose a tax 1
graded to the value of the wine, so that the popular |
restaurants of California should not have to pay as |
njuch on the claret served at meals as is imposed !
I upon champagne.
JThe chief defect of the law, however, is one to
which the revenue official made no reference. The
Call has repeatedly pointed out that by reason of the
delay of the courts in bringing to final adjudication
the controversy between the people and the express
and the telegraph companies the tax shirking cor
porations are daily engaged in extorting money from
their patrons in every part of the Union. To rem
edy that defect should be the first object of Congress,
for it is the one which works more injustice and is
most exasperating to the taxpayers.
; It is probable some of the Government officials
arc indifferent as to who pays the stamp taxes on tele
graph messages and express receipts so long as the
Government receives the money. It is as well to I
point out, therefore, that the Wells-Fargo Express
Company, at any rate, has been as willing to defraud
lie Government as to rob the people. As soon as
the tax was imposed that company made to The Call
ait; offer to receive from it a whole wagon-load of
packages as one package, thus cheating the national
revenue to that extent. It is not likely the offer was
made to The Call only. Probably a good many large
. s,hippers have taken advantage of it, and the total
loss of revenue by the trick may be considerable.
The war tax law was drawn up and enacted in a
hurry to meet' the needs of the Government at the
time. Experience has shown it to be defective in
many ways. Congress, therefore, not be con-.
: tent with amending it here and there. The whole law
, should be reviewed, revised and recast. The burdens
or taxation should be made as equal as possible upon
all sections and all classes, and there should be no
way open for tax-shirkers to extort money from the
public or to defraud the Government.
jWhen Mayor Pheian insisted that a military man
ir^st be placed on the local Democratic ticket he
probably did not think that his demand would be
taken as a declaration of war. The candidacy of
Major Geary has certainly created a wild, warlike
demonstration in the ranks of the Democratic faithful
I Win or lose, it seems to be the fashion the world
over to give naval officers something that will indi
cate the estimation in which they are held by their
country. In the United States they get swords; in
Spain they give them the ax. :'Â»rfÂ»v4

MOVABLE CHURCH-NOVEL
IDEA OF A PASTOR.
TWENTY OXEN DRAW THE PERAM
BULATING STRUCTURE FROM
ONE SPOT TO ANOTHER.
JAMESTOWN, Conanlcut Island, R. 1., Aug. 22.â Religious zeal has Improved
on the scriptural injunction to go into the highways and byways and compel sin
ners to attend church. The modern zealot brings a movable church to the highway
and byway wanderer and saves him the trouble of walking. The leader in this new
method of lifting the church out of the rut is the Rev. Charles Earl 1 reston, who
ls ln charge of St. Matthew's Church, on this island. Churches are few and far
between, and the people whom the good minister wishes to reach are scattered all
over the island. One church is sufficient for the inhabitants of several square
miles, but they will not come to church. Therefore the only thing to do is to take
the church to them. But how? Necessity in this emergency conceived the idea of
a movable church, and the Idea took tangible shape ln a structure that has just
made it- first trip, drawn by a team of oxen loaned by the pious farmers of that
locality.' The building, which is the invention of the Rev. Mr. Preston, is not a
structure of minute proportions, but a pretentious edifice, the furnishings of which
alone cost $3000. The audience room of the church measures 18x27 feet, and the
furniture is elaborate and beautiful. The building is mounted on a set of wheels
substantial enough to safely .bear the weight of the structure and with tires wide
enough to pass readily over any soft parts that may be encountered on the road
way. The first journey of the movable church was made without any accident or
injury to the building. The people assembled in great numbers to see the church
started on Its tour. Men and women came from Middletown and from the north
ern part of the Island, where all the native oxen, are owned, which, after being at
tached to the chapel, were ready for the start. There were twenty of these an
cient beasts of burden in line. C. H. Congdon of Rhode Island was the master of
ceremonies.
The rear wheels of the church had been Slightly elevated on planks, and as
soon as the brakes were off the building began to move, almost before the oxen
had straightened the chain. The bell was struck and the little building gracefully
rolled Into West street and after being pulled through the soft grass ground
turned up Cole street and rounded into Narragansett avenue. Down the remain
ing length of Narragansett avenue rolled the church, the tones of the bell pealing
out merrily. The doors and windows of dwellings were filled with eager faces and
the sidewalks were at times crowded by anxious persons who wanted to see "how
the thing would go." The first stop was made at a point where it was necessary
to wait for a lineman to cut a guy wire, which had caused the colors to be expe
ditiously lowered. Another halt was made soon after, but this time it was to re
move the detachable cross and ball so that the spire could pass under the telephone
wires. A lineman seated himself on the belfry and raised, one by one, the many
wires in places where they cross the roadway, until all such obstructions had been
successfully passed. ,". V -.?..'â ',';.
Once clear of the town, up hill and down dale rolled the movable church, the
nature of the roadway occasionally necessitating a little "jacking up" to prevent
accident, but beyond that no trouble occurred.
There are several springs of water on Conanlcut, and certain seasons of the
year the sidehllls show signs of moisture. A few of these spots were encountered
at the approach of what has recently been called Storks Hill. The hill surmount
ed, It was decided to locate on a site which had been offered by the Hon. Thomas
G. Carr, the owner of one of the oldest farms on the island. Luncheon was here
supplemented by what the ladies in this neighborhood provided for the helpers
The building was backed upon the lot and can be seen for miles around, both from
Narragansett Bay and the island of Rhode Island. Here it will remain until the
time for to be taken to the next resting place, and services will be held as fre
quently as seems well to the pastor. So much curiosity has been excited by the
novel church that there Is no possibility of audiences falling off for some time to
come. :>, : ',Q L >â 'â¢ '.;:

Wireless Telegraphy for Coast De
fense. ..'Â»â¢â â 
Santa Barbara Morning Press.
Among the scientific possibilities of the
near future are the extension of wireless
telegraphy to the needs of coast defense
and as a means of preventing collisions at
sea. Messages may be transmitted through
any kind of weather, and the wonderful
invention would be Invaluable In stormy
weather along a dangerous coast.
':-"U'- "?::'â â â â  Â« â¦ â  :
Marconi's Triumph.
Kansas City Journal.
The scientific achievement of transmit
ting messages long distances without wire
through the Marconi system must be re
garded as one of the most important of
Its class. Until Signor Marconi demon
strated his theory in reporting the yacht
race of last Thursday many people were
inclined to regard him as a good-natured
but unpromising visionary, pursuing an
idle but harmless phantom. But the, de
tails and result of the yacht race were
fully and accurately reported, and that
too in spite of, the fact that the weather
conditions were supposed to be least fa
vorable to the successful testing of the
new. invention. . When the vessels were so
faraway as to be lost in the haze their
movements were followed from Marconi's
dispatch boat, were transmitted within a
few, seconds to the New York Herald, and
thence throughout the country - * ... . - ..
The possibilities of this system which
1 require only that the delivering and re
: ceiving instruments be elevated sufficiently
to enable the sound waves to travel with
Â» out interruption from Intervening obsta
i cles, seem almost limitless. The use of
the system in the army and naval opera
tions is apparent at once, for it sets at
defiance the Interference of the enemy.

THE FATAL DAYâ S., City. The gen
eral belief is that Friday is the unlucky
or fatal 'day.- of "the week. A statistician
of the German Governemnt several years
ago went into farts and figures and de
veloped the fact that it is not Friday, but
Monday, that is the fatal or unlucky day.
His research showed that there were more
fatalities on Mondays than on any other
day of the week.

NORMAL SCHOOLSâ "W., Phllo, Cal.
The requirements for admission into the
Normal Schools outside of San Francisco
are that the applicant shall not be less
than 16 years of age, strong mentally,
morally and physically. To be admitted
without an examination must held a valid
teacher's certificate of any grade from
any county or city and county of the
State, or a diploma of graduation from a
California High School, or a diploma from
the ninth year of the public school. Ap
plicants presenting High School certlli
cates from other States may be admitted
without examination, at the discretion of
the faculty. Graduates from the ninth
year of the public schools of
California are admitted without ex
amination when the diploma is ac
companied by a statement as to standing
and special recommendation from teach
ers; record must show a high standing
on all subjects. Course four years.
For admission to the San Francisco
Normal School applicants must have been
graduated' by a High School accredited to
the University of California and must
have been recommended for admission to
that instltuion. Course two years.

THE CALL'S
HOME STUDY CIRCLE
Desk Studies for Girls.
This course has been specially arranged
for girls and young women who feel the need
of a more thorough knowledge of language,
books and money affairs. The studies will be
presented under the following heads:
1. NEW WORDS AND HOW TO USE THEM.
2. CORRESPONDENCE AND HANDWRITING.
3. READING COURSES AND A GIRL'S LIBRARY.
4. PERSONAL ACCOUNTS AND MONEY AFFAIRS.
5. WRITING FOR PUBLICATION.
Shop and Trade Studies for Boys
Boys and young men who are ambitious
to advance their positions will find that an
evening or two a week spent upon the
studies of this course will be of the largest
value. The work will be divided into five
departments, as follows :
1. ARITHMETIC OF THE WORKSHOP.
2. DRAWING FOR MECHANICS.
3. ARITHMETIC OF THE COUNTER AND OFFICE.
4. MONEY PROBLEMS AND BUSINESS RECORDS.
5. MACHINE DRAWING AND DESIGNS.

MANAGER HOME STUDY CIRCLE,
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, CALL BUILDING, SAN FRANCISCO, CAL.
TWO BIG CONVENTIONS
MAY BE BROUGHT HERE
CITIZENS ENTHUSIASTIC OVER THE IDEA
: |
Democrats and Republicans of Our ! City
Working Shoulder to Shoulder j -
for Success.
WITH the published announcement that there Is to be a meeting of the
Republican National Committee at "Washington early in December has
come increased interest in the project of securing the convention seat
of the tww great national parties for San Francisco in 1900. Thefproposi
tion has met with unqualified and universal indorsement on all sides,
and confidence is generally expressed that with proper effort San Francisco can
secure the distinction coveted.
Nearly every person who discusses the matter finds some new reason why San
Francisco should be given the prize, and not the least of these is the fact that
her growing Importance as a commercial entrepot in the past two years has been
so phenomenal as to attract widespread attention in the country and create an
intense curiosity to see the metropolis of the Pacific Coast and the States imme
diately, tributary to it.
Among the cities that are reported to be candidates for the honor of enter
taining the members of the conventions are Cleveland, Denver, Kansas City,
Milwaukee, Pittsburg, St. Louis, Cincinnati, Minneapolis and San Francisco, it
is said that Chicago will also contend for the votes of the committeemen.
J. J. Dwyer, Democratic National Committeeman from California, is out ot
town, and Is not expected to return for about two weeks, but his friends declare
that he will spare no effort with his colleagues in the interests of this city.
John D. Spreckels, the member of the Republican National Commi'-tee from
California, expects to leave for the East in about ten days, and in an interview
yesterday stated that he would begin missionary work on , the proposition at the
earliest opportunity. "I believe that If proper efforts are made," he said, it is
more than likely that the convention of the Republicans at least can be secured
for San Francisco. So far as 1 know there is no other city active in the matter
at the present time, except St. Louis. With concerted and well-directed action
1 believe it can be brought out here. When 1 was at St. Louis, during the last
convention, I heard expressions from many of the delegates that they wished
they were out on the coast, Instead of sweltering in the tropical heat where they
were.
"I shall attend the meeting of the National Committee and make every effort
to secure the convention for San Francisco. I believe that a large number of the
members will be In favor of this city on the ground of its climatic advantages
alone. Prompt action ought to be taken by the people here In view of the ear ly
day at which it is reported the meeting of the National Committee is to be held
for the purpose of selecting the convention seat.
"I expect to go East in about ten days, and shall do some preliminary work
Immediately after my arrival there. I shall see as many of the National Commit
teemen as possible and endeavor to win them over to the cause of San Francisco.
But much might also be done by having a representative delegation from, our
city present at the meeting of the committee."
F. W. Dohrmann, president of the Merchants' Association, after referring to
the great advantages that would undoubtedly accrue to the city and Sta*.Â», said:
"I think it is about time we should have these conventions out here. Perhaps
there never was a time when there was more likelihood of inducing them to
come to San Francisco If a strong effort were mad. in that direction. The atten
tion which San Francisco has attracted during the past two years has made many
people anxious to get acquainted with Californians in their homes and learn more
about our great State and the coast generally. They formerly considered us out
of the world. That San Francisco has grown strongly in interest to the people of
the East was very strikingly impressed on me during my trip to the East last
year, and this Interest has since been intensified. .
"Besides that, it seems to me that every American expects to come to Cali
fornia sooner or later. 1 never ask people whose acquaintance I make- in the
East 'Are you coming?' but 'When are you coming to California?! I, invariably
found that each one had it in mind to pay us a visit. I believe that -if the con
ventions or either of them were held in this city it would bring all these people
and thousands of others."
E. A. Denlcke, president of the Mechanics' Institute, Is a strong advocate of
the project. "It goes without saying." he remarked, "that It would be very
desirable to have. the national conventions meet in this city. We Have ample
accommodations for all who may come. Among other things, we have a very
large and comfortable assembly room In the Mechanics' Pavilion, so; there will
not be any need to erect one. California is at last beginning to receive that at
tention which is due her in a commercial sense and in every way. It ls very
desirable that the State of California should become known, especially to the
statesmen, so that her needs may be properly provided fur. which has not al
ways been the case heretofore."
"I am heartily in favor of the movement." said Horace Davis, the Republi
can candidate for Mayor. "A strong argument that could be used to Influence
the action of the Eastern members is our geographical position relative to our
new acquisitions In the Pacific. By coming here our political leaders would get a
clearer idea of the significance to San Francisco and the coast of our new* pos
sessions and their commercial importance. The holding of the conventions In
this city would bring the party leaders closer in touch and sympathy with our
needs and advantages. It is important to us and, to the country at large that
our prominence as a commercial center should be fully realized and appre
ciated. It Is certainly true that something ought to be done to turn the eyes
of the country toward California. "When you go East it is seldom you see any
notice In the newspapers of what is going on in California, the second largest
State in the Union, and which has advantages that are second to none."
Frank McLaughlin, chairman of the Republican State Central Committee,
when asked for an expression of opinion in regard to the matter, evinced great
interest in It by his look, but his evident ambition ls to be considered dead to the
world, as he is ln politics. "I decline to be Interviewed on this or any other
subject," he answered.
"But as chairman of the Republican State Central Committee, the people
of the party you represent and of the State generally naturally expect that you
would take a deep Interest in a matter of this kind, which Is' admitted to be of
great Importance to California. Tou certainly have some opinion '.n reference tc
the proposition," urged the reporter. .
"No; a word will you get from me. I simply decline to be interviewed on
any subject for any paper." And the major swelled up, smiled, swelled up
again and turned away. ' A - -
THE SAM FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, OCTOBER 14, 1899.
WINE-GROWERS
DID NOT SUFFER
FROM THE STORM
Were Favored by an
Early Season.
Â«.
LITTLE OF THE CROP WAS ON
THE VINES.
*
In Some Sections the Moisture Will
Be Beneficial â of the
Wine Men Are Brighter
Than for Years.
The fact that there was a short vintage
and that the grapes ripened early with
good saccharine strength, thus enabling
the producers to gather their crop
promptly was a matter of great good for
tune to the growers of wine grapes in
California this year.
Among those who were not acquainted
with the situation there was anxiety as
to the safety of the latter part of the
vintage when the recent unprecedented
storm swept over the- State. In ordinary
years the downpour would have done a
great deal of damage, but fortunately on
this occasion the producers wore prepared
for such a visitation and instead of the
storm being a detriment it will in some
cases doubtless prove a benefit. In speak
ing of the situation yesterday Secretary
rlotchkiss of the California Wine Makers'
Corporation said:
"1 don't think the storm has done any
particular Injury to the wine grapes, fori
the reason that in most districts the vint
age was practically over. This was the
case in Sonoma and Napa, while in Santa j
Clara the grapes still ungathered are of
varieties that will not suffer from the
wetting. In any event they would be all
right for distillation if gathered at once.
"In the Warm Springs section I believe
there Is still considerable of the -crop on I
the vines. However, they are zlnfand^ls, I
high in sugar strength, and the moisture I
will probably add to their productivity. !
Of course, if the storm should continue or I
cold and dampness prevail It would be j
necessary to handle the remainder of the |
crop in short order. Taking everything
Into consideration, the wine men have
every reason to be thankful that the
heavy rain did not come sooner."
At the present time the industry is ln
better condition as to prices and
prospects than It has been in
many years. The steadily grow
ing demand for California wines, the
gradual falling off In production and the
liberal offer of Henry J. Crocker to buy
all the dry wine grapes in the State for a I
term of seven years have tended to estab- 'â¢
lish a certain fixed value of wine vineyard
products, and as a result the producer and
merchant will fare unusually well this
year. The only cloud overshadowing the
Industry at the present time is the steady I
and destructive advance of the phyllox
era, but good prices will eventually cause !
the replanting of vineyards with resistant i
stock and bring the product up to the i
luture demand. .." r

ASSOCIATION WILL
BE MADE PERMANENT
DELEGATES FROM NORTH AND
SOUTH MEET HERE.
Basis of Representation for the Joint
Body of Wholesalers and Manu
facturers on the Coast Has
Been Fixed.
It is probable that steps will be taken
within the next few days to bring about a
permanent organization of the whole
salers, Jobbers and manufacturers of the
Pacific Coast. Yesterday a committee of
the Pacific Coast Traffic Association met
In Los Angeles with an organization of
the wholesalers and Jobbers of the south
ern city, formed during the present week.
The necessity for the Pacific Coast to act
as a unit in matters concerning the trans
portation of overland freights and the
control of trade territory resulted in the
sending of a delegation from this city to
Portland, Tacoma and Seattle recently.
That delegation fully accomplished what
was expected of It. and the report of the
proceedings in the Northwest has been
approved by the executive committee of
the Traffic Association.
The organization formed at Los Angeles
will undoubtedly work in harmony with
the wholesalers and jobbers of San Fran
cisco, Tacoma. Portland and Seattle.
A basis of the permanent organization
has been fixed and has been adopted by
the northwestern wholesalers and Job
bers. For every ten subscribers each city
will be permitted to send one delegate
to the meeting to be held in this city.
Then the delegates will finally settle all
matters relating to the plan of action.
The present Traffic Association was nec
essarily made temporary, for there were
no delegates from abroad.

NIGH MASONRY
CONFERS DONORS
ON ITS MEMBERS
Officers Elected for
Ensuing Term.
â â¦ â
CHARLES L. PATTON BECOMES
GRAND MASTER.
â : â¦
Report of the Trustees of the Home
at â Masonic Veterans
Choose Their New
Officers.
At the communication of the Grand
Lodge of Free and Accepted Masons yes
terday the board of trustees of the
Widows' and Orphans' Home at Decoto
presented a lengthy report of its steward
ship and a detailed account of the home
since it was opened on the Ist of May last
March for the reception of Inmates. This
shaws that the home up to date has cost
$136,350, $6842 having been spent for perma
nent improvements since the dedication
a year ago. To establish the home the
Grand Lodge donated one subscription of
$10,000. another of $20,000 and a third of
$7000 for furniture. Individual Masons,
Masonic bodies and Eastern Star chap
ters have raised for the home a sum
amounting in the aggregate to $102,500.
The recommendation of the board that
the per capita tax of $1 a year on every
member of the order for the maintenance
of the hame be continued was adopted by
a unanimous vote and the work of the
trustees In the establishing of the home
was Indorsed by a rising vote. The
board consists of Charles L. Patton, W.
Frank Pierce. William Johnston, Jacob
Voorsanger. Edward Peabody, Edwin
Whipple. Thomas Flint Sr., Carroll Cook
and William J. Landers, Since the opening
of the home there have been admitted to
It sixteen men, three women, ten boys
and six girls. In the report the board
pays a high compliment, to the superin
tendent, Dr. I. K. Akin, and to his wife,
the matron, and says that "they have
been model heads of our Masonic family."
In the afternoon there was an election
of grand officers for the ensuing term.
Charles L. Patton of San Francisco was
elected grand master; Joseph A. Fosnay
of Los Angeles deputy grand master,
William S. Wells of Martinez grand se
nior warden, Orin S. Henderson of Stock
ton grand junior warden, Edward Cole
man of San Francisco re-elected grand
treasurer and George Johnson of San
Francisco re-elected grand secretary.
The Masonic Veterans' Association has
elected the following named as its offi
: cers for the ensuing term: Henry F. Wil
liams, grand president; William F. Low-
I den. linn grand vice president; Edward
F. Preston, second grand vice president;
Edward A. Sherman, grand secretary;
James L. Cogswell, grand treasurer;
Joseph 11. Wythe, grand chaplain; Hiram
N. Rucker, grand orator; Lewis A. Spit
ler. grand marshal; J. T. Apperson, grand
standard-bearer; Henry S. Lubbock,
second grand standard-bearer; Samuel
W. Levy, third grand standard-bearer;
W. W. Sutton and George C. Kennedy,
grand stewards; Charles W. . Mitchell,
grand pursuivant; Treat P. Clark, grand
tyler.
OVERDUE SHIPS ARE
CAUSING UNEASINESS
REINSURANCE PAID ON THE
CHARLES E. MOODY.
Premium Also Offered on the St.
Enocn The German Warship
Geier Will Return Here.
Some anxiety is felt in shipping and
Insurance circles over the American ship
Charles E. Moody and the British ship
St. Enoch. The former is now out 179
days from Norfolk. Va., for Honolulu
with a cargo of coal for the Government
and 45 per cent reinsurance Is being paid
on her. The St. Enoch is % days out
from Panama for San Francisco and 15
per cent 1;? being paid to reinsure her
chances.
The Moody is owned in San Francisco, j
having recently been purchased by Lewis,
Anderson & Co. She is a fine wooden
vessel and well found to cope with any j
kind of weather. It is her cargo of coal j
that the underwriters fear, however. The ;
St. Enoch Is coming here from Panama
In ballast, and judging from the pas
sages made by other vessels from that
port she is not very much overdue.
The German man-of-war Geier is to re- |
turn to San Francisco. She will leave I
Vancouver, B. C, on the 18th Inst, for
this city and after a short stay will pro
ceed to Central America.
The bark Alden Besse arrived last night
from Honolulu after a fine passage of 17
days. The barkentines Planter and S. x.
Castle were 29 and 28 days respectively
making the run, so the showing made
by the Alden Besse is a particularly good
one.

at Coos Bay for over a week. The chan
nel has shifted and the collier is draw

J. F. ("Jack") Hawkins, who for years
did such excellent work for the Mer
chants' Exchange as one of Its staff of
reporters at Melggs wharf, has gone into
business for himself. He ana F. P.
Christian- have opened an office ln Seattle
as shipping and Custom-house brokers
and are doing well. Christian was for
merly a Custom-house Inspector, and is
well up in that line of business,' while
what Hawkins does not know about ship
ping is not worth learning. "Jack" was
orn and raised on North Beach and his
host of friends all join in wishing him
success.
Runaway on Taylor Street.
Yesterday afternoon a team of horses
belonging to the Morton Drayage Com
pany, while standing on Taylor street,
near Kills, took fright at a passing ve
hicle and darted down Taylor street at a
mad rate, demolishing several posts on
its way. On arriving near Turk street its
course was suddenly checked by collid
ing with a delivery wagon belonging to
Faber & Co., grocers, which was stand
ing in front of their store at the time.
The wagon was smashed into splinters by
the force of the collision and the horse
so badly 'injured that it had to be de
stroyed. The runaway team escaped
without damage.
St. Peter's and St. Paul's.
Last evening was "ice cream Â«iight" at
the St. Peter's and St. Paul's bazaar in
Washington-square Hall. Every one had
to eat ice cream to be In the style, and
Mrs. Spadina. who presided, assisted by
her daughters,' was kept busy. Vocal mu
sic yielded pleasure to the many attend
ants. Thomas Smith sang a coon son,<,
and Master Martin Kreling showed how
to walk for a cake. This evening the
Pennsylvania military band will play. The
bazaar will continue one more week.
Declined to Reindict.
The Grand Jury yesterday refused to re-
Indict P. McAran and M. C. Hayes for
perjury. McAran and Hayes went on tho
bond of Eddy Brooks, -who was accused
of grand larceny, and were Indicted by
the last Grand Jury. They filed a demur
rer, which was sustained by Judge Law
lor. to the effect that the Indictment was
defective, but lie refused to dismiss tha
defendants. It appears that the piece of
property of which they claimed to be
joint owners is owned by Hayes alone. m

The annual championship regatta of the
Pacific- Association of the Amateur Ath
letic Union will be held to-morrow over
the El Campo course. The starting line
will be to the south of tin- steamer
I'kiah's landing place, and the course will
be in a northerly direction past the danc
ing pavilion one and a sixteenth miles to
the stakes, the finishing line being in full
view of the crowd on the beach. A good
list of entries has been received, though
there are no representatives from the
Stockton Athletic Association, the Olym
pic Boat Club or the University of Cali
fornia. The Dolphins have not entered a
barge crew, but have contributed three
â¢ -scullers. In the junior skiff race
there are four entries, and In the senior
skiff event there are three. The latter,
the entries in which are A. W. Pape, Dr.
i.e. Dennis and George Baker, will prob
ably be the most exciting ami keenly con
tested event of the day, all the men being
in excellent condition and hard workers.
'1 he two first named have had a wide ex
perience In boat racing.
For the senior shell race there are three
entries, and the same Dumber for the
junior shell race. In the former event J.
P. Foley of the South Ends Is likely to
prove the winner, and in the latter George
Lewis of the Alameda Boat Club. The

South Ends are entered in the senior

likely to win.
The South End junior barge crew is in
good trim and consists of strong men well
trained. It will probably win the junior
championship. If it does so it will row
over the course for the intermediate
barge championship. Several of the. con
testants are entered in two or more
events, Dr. C. C. Dennis being in the
senior skiff, junior and intermediate barge
races; .1. P. Foley pulling in the senior

G. Greenleaf rowing in the senior and
junior barge crews of the Alameda. Club,
and E. B. Hade of Alameda being en-
rod in the senior shell race and also in
the senior baige event. F. W. Ayers not
being able to secure a boat to his liking
has not entered in the senior skiff event.
in the junior barge race the Ariel crew
consists entirely of men entered in that
event only, whereas the Alameda and
South End crews have two men each who
j-.ro entered for other events. This ought
to be a deoiderl advantage to the Ariels,
who Iwv n practicing faithfully and
doing go.hi work.

COACHES AND ELEVENS
HARD AT THEIR WORK
University of California Football Players Will Meet the Olympics
To-Day for the Second Time.
/' I '>HE public which follows football had an opportunity to
1 ?auge both the college teams and their mutual competitor;
THE Olympics. The prevalent opinion is that the Unlver
gauge both the college teams and their mutual competitor,
the Olympics. The prevail nt opinion is that the Univer-
I sity of California has the strongest team In the field and
â  at this early day of the season 13 In a position which
should result in a victory on Thanksgiving day, when Blue and
Gold crosses Cardinal.
Assurance often means victory, but a talk with Conches
Ccchran and Kelly produces no such confidence In California's
success. Stanford has just begub a season In which everything
will be formative and evolutionary. Chamberlin is coach par
excellence for just such work, and every day something is done
ward the making of a team at Palo Alto. To say that Cali
fornia has an unshakable hold on the intercollegiate game is
reckoning without the host. A; much stronger and better game
will have to be played by the Dim- and Gold to win the great
yearly contest than has been played during the last week.
Nothing will really be known of the ability of the Cardinal
eleven until two weeks before the big game. At that time a
Stanford team will be formed and will play as the eleven rep
resenting the college. Until then every Stanford team seen in
this city Will be an experimental one. This may or may not
be a disadvantage. Cochran took raw material last year, de
veloped it and created a team that stuck together like mucilage.
Coach Chamberlain is doing the same with Stanford, and if
matters progress there with the steady improvement the work
already shows the Cardinal will have a team worth;.- of any
California can put in the field, j
A slump has struck Berkeley and Cochran is by no means
satisfied with the team's work; During the week "Locomotor"
Smith. Kaarsberg, VVomble and Hall were laid off on account
of slight injuries and a too early rounding Into condition. Cali
fornia ls coming into shape ratjier rapidly, and the trainers are
exercising the greatest watchfulness over the men in regard to
physical condition. The practice game on the California
campus has been slow, comparing it with that which the
coaches think it should be. Ccebran, since Kelly's advent, has
been giving most of his time to the team play, with occasional
advice to linesmen. Kelly is hÂ»idling the backs, and the Cali
fornia players have discovered that there were several things
in back-of-the-llne play they had not learned.
Whipple's injury will keep him out of the game for some
time, and his position at tackle is now being played by different
men. When Smith, Womble, Kaarsberg and Hall were taken
out of the game the substitute men were given a chance, and
for a time everything was chaotic on the California team.
These four men will probably play in the game this afternoon,
as Cochran is strong in the belief that the team which is likely
to meet Stanford on Thanksgiving day should receive as much
practice in match games as possible. California's men will be
in no better condition to-day than when the Olympics met them
two weeks ago In the opening game of the season.
The Olympics' confidence of an unbroken string of victories
has given way to a hopefulness for a victory. "With material
that is equal to the best they have been vanquished by Califor
nia, an experienced but light team, and tied by Stanford, inex
perienced and also light in weight. The clubmen are beginning
to think that Individuals are not everything and something
must be done to shake off the "hoodoo." This week the men
have trained with more spirit, and the public is promised that
the team will know its signals without painful waits. Some
changes will be made in the line-up of the team, In the first
half Varney and Dinsmore will play halves and Thlerkauf full
back. In the second half Felton Taylor will play fullback,
Thierkauf probably going to one of the end positions. On the
line a few changes will have the effect of making a new forma
tion. Monroe will occupy the central position, with Cadwallader
stump of the log, and expects to have his
patient running around freely hi a few
Weeks. ... t
J. B. Martin, the noted fox terrier ox
pert, has refused the invitation of the
California Collie Club to judge that breed
at Oakland. He does not care to counte
nance shows held under any other than
American Kennel Club rides. Mr. Mar
tin's action is warmly applauded by the
fanciers of the coast.
A movement has been instituted by the
StÂ» Bernard Club in petitioning the San
Francisco Kennel Club to appoint L. L.
Campbell superintendent, as he commend
ed himself to exhibitors at the late show
by his impartial enforcement of the rules.
It is unlikely that tin- San Francisco
Kennel Club will accede to the request
of the convention of specialty clubs to
hold the next show in April. It is con
sidered that the uncertain weather would
endanger its financial success. Besides
which none but short-haired dogs would
be in good coat and fit for exhibition.

nard, Alta Maud, by Dictator out of Alta

rises with the yeast in it.
F. O. YOUNG EQUALS WORLD'S RECORD
FOR TEN SHOTS WITH A REVOLVER
FO. YOUNG, a member of the Columbia Pistol and Rifle
Club of this city, has. equaled the world's revolver rec
ord. Some weeks since Thomas Anderton, the expert
_ revolver shot of the East, made the phenomenal score
' â¢ of 35 points In ten shots on the Columbia target ln a
80-shot match. His score equaled 96 out of a possible 100 points
on the standard American target, which Is brilliant shooting.
F. O. Young, one of a -little .group of experts in this city, an
nounced at once that he would try for the record at the first
opportunity. Tills developed last Sunday at Harbor View
range.
Mr. Young made a match to shoot his .4 S. & W. Russian
model revolver against P. Becker and G. Hoadley, who used tar
get pistols. In his ten shots at fifty yards on the Columbia
target with a four-inch bullseye Mr. Young made 1, 6, 2, 3, 6, 4,
3, 2, 1, 7â35 pointsâ equals Anderton's record and beats'
the Pacific Coast record of 41, held by J. E. Gorman. Out of
the ten shots seven of the bullets wore placed within the four
inch circle, while the average was 3.5 inches. The poorest shot
was 3Vfc inches from the dead center. ', : i

STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Oct. 13.â With the cooler
weather there has come a revived interest upon the gridiron,
and, excepting Rice, all of Stanford's varsity players are none
the worse for their brush with the Olympics last Saturday.
From now on tin- coaches will devote more time to the fine
points of the game, and when chamberlin next sends his men
to the city it will be as a team rather than a mere aggregation.
This week has seen the addition of two men to the line
which will materially stiffen and strengthen it. "Babe" Bur
nett, the giant center of the 1897 team, has donned his suit
again after a week of training under the care of Dr. Murphy
and is in fairly good condition, tipping the beam at 190 pounds.
It is probable he will be seen at tackle this year, as Cham
berlin is desirous of playing him there. In this case Ed Gilman
will play the center position, as he has the necessary weight
and experience. The other man is De Forrest, for several years
guard on the Multnomah team of Portland and one of the best
players in the north. His position is guard,' where he will be
placed immediately by the coaches, who have been waiting for
euch an acquisition to the team. The ends will be well looked
after, but back of the line the outlook is not so promising.
Garth Parker, a veteran at the end of the line, is being tried
at half, and though he Is quick he has the unpardonable fault
of running back - with the ball. Until he overcomes this he
has poor prospects of becoming such a ground gainer as the
position calls for. The other half will undoubtedly be a fresh
man, as will also be the fullback. Roberts, Campbell, Jordan
and Willot are candidates for the latter place, while Wilson,
Smith, Allen, Raitt and Parkin are in the race for the former.
The trainers and coaches mingle with their men both on the
field and off It. and are coming to know just what work each
man can do. The early establishment of the training table
and the constant supervision of a skilled trainer precludes the
danger of fever which was so current among the squad last
year.
The past week has been given up partially to interclass
games, which have done much to bring out the crowd and de
velop enthusiasm. As the time for the freshman intercollegi
ate match draws near the baby team continues to show im
provement. The work of the team is good, considering that its
personnel is changed with each game. They have the best ma
terial that has come Into the university since the class of '9S
entered, and there is no reason why the men should not give
a good account of themselves before the season is. over. How
ever, they have much to accomplish yet before they are a
strong team, and the attention of the coaches 'must be directed
toward them for the next two weeks if they hope for victory.
TAIL-ENDERS
WILL MEET IN
A HOT FIGHT
This Is the week in which the balltossers
of Oakland are going to try the great act
of forcing the Crabs of Santa Cruz into
last place. The Duties are inflated with
recent success and are imbued with the
notion that nothing can stop them, so for
midable have they proved of late. On
the other hand, Manager Tuttle of Santa
Cruz attributes the comparatively poor
showing of his aggregation to nothing
but hard luck. The fact is that Santa
Cruz has been suffering from weakness In
the box. Whelan has been pitching win
ning ball, but Andrews and Balsz appear
to have lost their cunning. Tuttle has
strengthened his team by substituting
McCarthy for Williams at second base,
and he thinks that he now possesses the
fastest Infield in the State. McCarthy
was formerly the mainstay of the Wat
sonville club. Besides being a good
striker, he is capable of playing any posi
tion on the diamond.
- Oakland and Santa Cruz will play two
games in this city and one in Oakland on
Sunday morning, while the local team will

THREATENING
WEATHER IS
DUCK WEATHER
Hence Gunmen Go Joyously
to Their Preserves This
Week.
With the thermometer registering over
| 100 degrees the gunners who went in quest
j of ducks last Sunday enjoyed but little
I sport to compensate them for their exer
tions. The members of the Teal and
I Ibis clubs did no shooting, while there
was but little organized shooting on any
of the preserves. Those who did secure
birds did not enjoy them, as they would
not stand transportation. Down about
Alvarado no birds were to be seen.
Although the week opened with poor
firomise of sport for some time to come,
t closed with brightened prospects.
Threatening weather is duck weather, and
the elements have done more than threat
en since Wednesday. As a sequence the
clubmen will seek their preserves joy
ously this week and expect well-filled
game bags. The district over which men
may shoot will be extended, as ground
that has been dry will have filled with
water, thus forming new ponds.
Reports received from Southern Oregon
are to the effect that enormous numbers
of birds are to be found there. These will
ln time come southward and will spread
out over this country, providing sensa
tional sport later on.
A. M. Shields was the. most successful
man last Sunday on the Prlngle Club's
preserve near Suisun, securing eighteen
birds, sixteen of them being sprig.
The men on the Olympic Gun Club pre
serve last Sunday were: George Walker.
Dr. A. T. Derby, H. L. Miller, Howard
Black, W. A. Marshall and Fred Feudner.
Despite the heat, they tried the sport, the
average bag being about five birds.
On Wednesday the sport was better,
owing to the marked change in the
weather. Al Gumming: secured eleven
sprig, four mallard and a white goose.

ROAD RACE POSTPONED.
The annual ten-mile handicap road race
of the Associated Cycling Clubs, which
was scheduled to be run to-morrow over
the straightaway course from Fruitvale
to Haywards, has been postponed for one
week owing to the Inclement weather.
The racing board decided that this was
the best plan, as it is hard on the riders
and somewhat dangerous, to run a race
over a muddy road, and besides the men
will be afforded another week in which
to train for the event. The entries al
ready number over three score and more
will undoubtedly come in now that it ha*
been postponed. v nas

Local Leashmen Will Strive to
Secure Next Year's Clas
sic Event.
The coursing men of California will
make a strong effort to have the Ameri
can Waterloo, one of the classic events
in the coursing year, held in this State
next season. The stake will be run tnis
year on the 20th inst. on an inclosed
field at Dallas, Texas. Only two Califor
nia dogsâ Emm Pasha and Metallicâ of
Pasha Kennels will be in the ' running.
John Halton, who has gone on to" the
meeting, Is commissioned to invite the
American Coursing Board to run off its
greatest event in this State next year.
The sport on . this coast has outgrown
that of the East. While the added money
for the American Waterloo is only $600
Ingleslde and Union Park each add near
ly $800 or more every week in the year.
Union Park adds over $1000 this week to
mark its one hundredth coursing meeting.
One hundred and forty-four dogs will go
to the silos' ln two stakes. It seems each
week that the highest class dogs have
been entered, but this stake towers above
all others in quality.
Ingleslde Coursing Club was largely
represented on Thursday at the funeral of
John Eagan, an obi time coursing man.
Mr. Eagan owned stJme notable perform
ers, the. test of them being Mayflower.
He also owned Sly Girl, another brilliant
performer, before coursing had developed
into a business proposition. Of the dogs
which ran in his name in recent years
Billy Gladstone was considered the best.
He was the runner-up In last year's Pro
duce stake.
Sterl & Knowles will start Frosty
Morn, their latest Australian Importation,
at Union Park this week. He is a brin
dle dog of good size and quality and
should equal the performances of the
same firm's imported Sleety Mist on the
field.
The most notable sale of greyhounds
ever made on this coast was that effected
by.D. J. Heaiy of Petaluma during the
past week. He disposed of six of his
kennel for $2000. The dogs Include Rusty
Gold, O'Hara, For Glory, Amarosa, Oleta
and Ripple. All except the latter two
have been thoroughly tried out and have

formers. The dogs are running in the.
name of Sterl & Knowles, but the pur
chaser is said to be a woman who has
taken. a deep interest in coursing. Before

Tim Cromn $600 for Wild Tra.ee or $1000
for Wild Tralee and Thornhill. The of
fer was declined.
This sale Indicates that the commercial
side of coursing Is just beginning to as
sume prominence and old time ieashraen
find it difficult to embrace the new order
of affairs. In the past money was sel
dom paid for dogs, most of them being
secured in trade or as gifts. â¢ .

DOT IN DOLLARS
State's Annual Loss
Is Immense.
â A,
IRRIGATORS WILL CONVENE
â¢â¦
CONVENTION TO HEAR EXPERT
TESTIMONY.
Â«
Placer County Furnishes an Example
of the Benefits of Impounded
Water iv a Dry
Season.
An attempt will be made at the coming
convention In this city of the California
State Association for the Storage of {
Fiood Waters to show just how many
millions of dollars tho State wastes every '
ten years by permitting its water supply ;
t ) run away. This novel computation
will have interest for all classes. Leading
men In the movement are having the prob
lem figured out. There are difficulties in
the way of making exact figures, and
probably nothing better than a reason
able approximation can be given, but this
will be valuable In a general way.
Among the difficulties to be met In the
calculation are included different soils,
which absorb water in varying degrees;
tbe amount taken up by vegetation; also,
in computing the value of water that Is
finally determined to be lost, the price of
water must be averaged. In some places
the price of m miner's inch of water Is
higher than in other sections. It will be ,
possible to get a fair average price, and '
the. miner's Inch will be the basis of mak
i!,g up. the valuation of the lost water.
When the number of millions of dollars
wasted can be shown, some of the advo
cates of saving the water think it will be
easy to demonstrate to all persons who
lend money upon the basis of rural realty;
and especially th, bankers, that saving
the water would be a good investment.
fcolely from the standpoint of insuring the
ability of the borrowers of money in the
country to repay the principal and the in- !
terest of loans.
The Government has gathered statistics
concerning the annual escape of flood
water from the Ohio and the Missis
rivers, and the figures are surprising. The
California problem will have to be figured
out on a somewhat different basis. The
cost of restraining the flood waters and
the money to be earned, on the average,
by having a supply of water that will per
mit the regular irrigation of vast tracts
of land, without regard to the drvnt of
the season, will be included in the" compu
tation to be submitted.
Colonel John P. Irish says that the dif
ference between irrigation and the lack of
irrigation was Illustrated fully last year.
The San Joaquin Valley, owing to the
scanty rain of the preceding winter, had
a very small crop. Placer County, on the
other hand, had a full crop of fruit, be
cause the South Yuba C mpany had the
Fordycc reservoir full of water" that had
been stored. When the South Yuba ditch
ran dry the company turned in the
water from the reservoir, and the people
had all the water they needed and the
orchards were correspondingly fruitful.
That demonstrated what could be done,
Most of the people of Placer County had
forgotten that â¢here was such a "water
supply In the reservoir until the emer
gency due to the dry season made it
necessary to draw upon it.

BREAKFAST ON DRINK.
Coffee Makes Many Dyspeptics.
"CoffÂ«v? and I had quite a tussle. Two
years ago I was advised by the doctor
to quit the use of coffee, for I had a
chronic case of dyspepsia and serious
nervous troubles, which did not yield
to treatment. I was so addicted to eof- j
fee that it seemed an impossibility to
quit, but when I was put on Postum !
Cereal Food Coffee there was no trou- .
ble In making the change, and to-day
I am a well woman.
"One of the lady teachers in our pub- ;
lie schools was sick and nervous. Fre-
quently the only thing she took for
breakfast was a cup of coffee. I urged
her to try leaving off the coffee and
use Postum Instead. Went so far ns
to send her a sample from my box and
give her directions. She now uses
nothing but Postum Food Coffee, and
told me a short time ago that she was
perfectly well. : : -'.-

water and after it comes up to a boll
S e e ...it from that time on it bolls fif-
teen or twenty minutes; then use good
cream and you have a drink that would
be relished by the Queen." â Mrs. Lizzie
Whlttaker, Kidder, Mo. Postum is sold
by all first-class grocers at 15 and 25
