DUSKY QUEENS
IN A NEW ROLE
AT THE ORPHEUM
IT was a long time ago that Solomon 'â 
said there was nothing new under the
sun. Since then the Hawaiian Queens
have appeared.
Orpheum patrons until this week have
asked the wherefore of their name. The!
Queens have at last explained by pre- j
senting "King Moo's Wedding Day."
It takes a good opera glass to convince
one that these are the same three who j
last week tripped forth Frenchlly, as ;
daintily artificial as Louis XIV fan pic- j
tures should be. Now they are dusky of j
cheek, red of mouth and lithe of limb. j
They love and they hate and they are j
beautiful, warm barbarians. .
The second place of honor falls to the i
Florcnz troupe of acrobats. There is a
happy family spirit apparent among them
that makes their work more cheerful than
l the average performance of that class.
I There Is no nervous, impending music to
lead up to great feats; they do wonders !
on each other's shoulders in a casual way
and come right side up again in a twink- |
ling with a "How-dy-di expression.
Then off they go after a grand lobbing i
and hopping all over the stageâ lt looks .â¢
for all the world like a frog festival.
Mr and Mrs. Harold Haskins present
what they call a "legitimate comedy." â¢
The legitimate is an elastic affair. The
Homeos and the Parthenias like to dis
tinguish themselves from Amelia Bing
ham and Rose Coghlan by claiming legiti- :
ma< v. and they In turn wave the word over
the heads of vaudeville stars, and now
come the Harold Haskinses to stalk supe
rior to ethers on the same boards. But |
their farce is a roaring funny one when j
seen for the first time, and you laughed | '
at It last night, be you young or be you
old, even though to-day's sober judgment j
frown at the folly.
The rest of the programme is made up of j ,
last week's holdovers and some fairly; '
good new turns. Little Elsie dances and j
imitates with wiry black arms dangling
in shoulder gloves. She is less fat and
comfy looking than the other stage babies !
who have been descending of late days
upon the town; but then she Is more
clever than most of them. Doing a
prodigy turn is warranted to reduce
weight. Try It.
SARAH COMSTOCK.
Nance O'Neil in "The Jewess."
If a tragedienne desires to test the full
capacity of her dramatic temperament let ;
her appear in the "curse scene" of "The
Jewess" and she will have every oppor- j
tunity to prove whether she is endowed
with versatility. In this part of the drama j
the actress is called upon to Interpret a
variety of emotions, the contrast and |
fervor of which demand the keenest ap
preciation of dramatic climaxes. Hence
it is this very scene which seals the fate
of that woman who desires to be regarded
as a good "Jewess." If she fails here
then all her activity in the balance of the
play becomes futile.
Nance O'Neil seems to dissect the lines. j
She knows every iota of their import,
and what is more she is aware of the |
uniqueness of the Jewish character. She i
does not only portray the emotions of the
woman, the love and hate of th" deserted
one, but she pictures accurately the j
despair of the faithless child who has j
wandered from the straight path of her
religion, her people, her nation, for a
man whom she thinks unworthy of such
sacrifice. Her despair 13 pictured In her i
features, her tears are not affected, her
hate is fierce and her curse springs from
her lips with the piercing anguish of a
soul In torture. She acts her part In a
manner which entrances the audience and
rivets its attention until the curtain drops.
No wonder that five curtain calls re
warded her for her splendid work.
However. Edwin Mordant is not a fit
leading man for such a virile actress as
Nance O'Neil. He may be well enough
as a juvenile In a passably good society j
play, but ho Is certainly out of place In
a drama like "The Jewess." His voice is !
not endowed with sufficient dramatic tim- â 
bre, nor does his deportment exhibit suf- ;
ficient force. The contrast between him I
and the tragedienne is too great to be
; passed by unnoticed.
j Stage children art generally swallowed
up in a play of this sort, but little Anita
! Loos did her part so unaffectedly and
with such natural grace that it "would
be a pity to deprive her of the credit due
her. A stage child who is really child
like in a play is a rarity, but Anita may
be classed among these exceptions. "The
Jewess will be repeated this evening.
ALFRED METZGER.
WIFE- BEATER PUNISHED.
William Fleming, a Coal Carter, Sent
to the County Jail for Four
Months.
William Fleming, river of a coal
wagon, living at 60&-4 Clementina street,
appeared before Judge Treadwell yester
day on a charge of battery upon his
wife. She was In court and had a pair
of blackened eyes and her face was
otherwise bruised and swollen. After
testifying to the assault she begged the
Judge to be lenient with her husband as
she had five young children and had no
means of supporting them.
The Judge said that he could not allow
such a brute to go free and he was
sorry that the law did not empower him
to give him 500 lashes and compel him
to keep sober and support his wife and
children. He sentenced him to four
months in the County Jail and gave Mrs.
Fleming a $5 goldplece to buy provisions
for herself and children and promised
to take her to the Associated Charities,
so that she could be looked after during
her husband's incarceration.
Bonds Insufficient.
The bonds accepted by Judge Treadwell
and Clerk Rice of Judge Conlan's court
for the release of Harry Walters, Henry
Roberts and James West, three bunko
men charged with swindling William
Schneider of Honolulu out of $105, were
declared Insufficient by Bond Expert New
man yesterday, and the three men were
ordered Into custody by Judge Mogan. The
bondsmen were Milo Waldron, saloon
keeper. 914 Market street, and James Til
ton, saloon-keeper, 1001 Market street.
Charles Crawford, the other bunko man
arrested, had the charge of grand larceny
against him dismissed by Judge Mogan, as
Schneider Called to Identify him, but he
is still held on the charge of vagrancy. '
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, OCTOBER 11, 1599.
CANADA SHUT OUT
FROM TIDE WATER
Text of the Agreement
as to. Alaska.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. WELLING
TON HOTEL, WASHINGTON, Oct. 13.
--considerable gratification is felt in ad
ministration circles at the announcement
made by Sir Louis Davies that Canada
has agreed to the arrangement establish
ing a temporary boundary line between
the Dominion and Alaska determined on
by Secretary Hay and Mr. Tower several
weeks ago.
No official confirmation of Sir Louis
Davies' statement was received by the
State Department up to the close of of
fice hours this afternoon. Nevertheless
advices received by the authorities dur
ing the past few days convince them that
he has been correctly quoted, and that
the modus Vivendi has been agreed upon.
It has been practically settled for sev
eral weeks, but there remained some
minor matter of detail to be perfected,
and their discussion has delayed final
agreement.
- As it will be signed, the modus vlvendl
will provide that a temporary boundary
line shad be drawn from the connecting
Canadian custom-house at White Pass
and Chllkoot Pass. The line shall then
be traced southward to a mountain peak
about a mile and a half northwest of the
Indian village of Kuckwan. That line
shall determine the beginning and end of
the authority of the two governments
over their respective territory, and It
shall not prejudice any claim either may
have to territory on either side of the line
outside of such expressed authority. The
rights of private individuals shall not be
prejudiced ln consequence of their loca
tion either in American or British terri-
tory.
The modus vlvendl, Intended specifically
to prevent a conflict of authority between
the constabulary of the two governments
and strife between the citizens of the
United States and the subjects of Great
Britain shall continue in force until a
treaty is negotiated determining a perma
nent boundary.
By this agreement Canada will be ex
cluded from tidewater arid all her goods
sent to the Dominion from the Pacific
must pass through American territory.
She will have no outlet on Lynn canal and
the location of the line above Kluckwan
places her out of reach of even canoe nav
igation. The matter of permitting a Ca
nadian warehouse at tidewater and the
transit of goods to the Dominion over
American territory has no place in the
negotiations relating to the temporary
boundary line and ls a subject of separate
discussion. - .â¢ .-:â¢. â¢â¢ ;
The establishment of the modus vlvendl
shows how carefully Secretary Hay has
safeguarded the Interests of this country'
and especially the Pacific Coast.
SUISSER'S PLEA
IS NOT GUILTY
Slayer of Sheriff Farley
in Court.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SALINAS. Oct. 13. âAt 10 O'clock this
morning the Superior courtroom was
crowded by citizens of Salinas, who as
sembled to be present when the murderer
of Sheriff Farley should be brought Into
court. At the appointed time Sheriff Keef
ushered George Suisser Into the court
room, and as he entered he cast one nerv
ous glance at the spectators and then
hurriedly took a seat in the prisoners'
dock. I'pon Judge Dorn calling the case
C. A. O Dell, attorney for {he prisoner,
made a strong plea to be allowed until
Monday for bis client to plead, but on
his falling to give satisfactory reasons for
a continuance the Judge Instructed that
the plea would be taken at ! o'clock this
afternoon whether the attorney was ready
or not, and continued the case to tlrat
time.
At 2 o'clock the courtroom was again
crowded to hear what plea the assassin
would make. As Sheriff Keef conducted
him to his seat he walked with more forti
tude than in the morning, and entered a
plea of not guilty. The trial was set fur
Tuesday.

SCHNEIDER AND
CUIGNET FIGHT
Due! Resulting From the
Dreyfus Case.
Special Dispatch to ThÂ« Call.
[ LONDON, Oct. 13.â A dispatch from
| Paris says that a representative of the
i Intransigeant, M. Rochefort's paper, as
serts that while seeing a friend off on the
Orient express last evening he observed
Colonel Schneider, former military at
tache of the Austro-Hungarian Embassy
at Paris, being carried to the train, ac
companied by physicians, and with blood
j flowing from a wound In his side. Colonel
Schneider said: : . â ;
"I recently returned from Switzerland
i to fight a duel with Captain Cuignet on
account of hi-" evidence before- the Rennes
j court-martial."
j Captain Cuignet, ln the course of his
! evidence at Rennes in the second trial of
; Dreyfus, referred to a dispatch of Colonel
: Schneider, denouncing as a forgery a let
| ter purporting to have been written by
! him in which he was represented as al
i luding to efforts made by the German and
I Italian military attaches to conceal their
j relations with Dreyfus. This allegation
' of forgery Captain Cuignet ridiculed, de
i claring tnat if Colonel Schneider per
sisted in his attitude the French general
staff was in a position to prove beyond
i dispute the authenticity of the letter
which Schneider had repudiated. He then
added:
"The author of the letter Is, I know, of
very inferior position as compared with
his friends (Schwartzkoppen and Panlz
zardl). Perhaps he cannot do otherwise
than deny this. But he must know that
his letter is not the only one of his com
munications now ln our hands."
BIGAMIST ROBERTS
VISITING BROOKLYN
Congressman-Elect Declares He Vio
lates No Law in Maintaining
Three Households.
NEW YORK. Oct. 13.âCongressman
elect Brigham Henry Roberts from Salt
j Lake City, who is in Brooklyn, came East
over a week ago on a private visit. He
1 will remain about a week longer and then
| go to Philadelphia and probably to Wash
ington. Speaking to a reporter he said
: there was no indictment against him when
i he left Utah that he, knew of, nor did he
! know there was now an indictment. He
had broken no law, he said. That he had
a plurality of wives was true, but he had
married them before the manifesto of lS'.'O.
There had been no plural marriages since
i then, nor would there be any. Any in
| fringement of the statute would be discip
lined by the church as quickly as by the
laws of" the land.
Mr. Roberts said his visit to Brooklyn
I was merely a friendly one to the people of
his own religious faith. They were here
' to preach the gospel, and did not desire to
! be brought into any controversy which
might affect him.
Speaking of Ids election to Congress he
said it had been accomplished independ
ently of the church, as he had received
: the "greater number of votes from Gen
; tiles, and it had been an election by the
people. He had no fear about facing
Congress nor any misgiving in regard to
j the outcome of the situation.
DOMINICANS RELEASED
FROM CUBAN PRISON
Denounce Their - Imprisonment as
Worse Than Any Spanish
Outrage.
SANTIAGO DE CUBA, Oct. 13.â A party
of thirty-two Dominicans, who had been
confined at Baracoa for two months, were
released yesterday and arrived here to
day. They were members of the Jlmenes
expedition, which was captured because
its object was not concealed, as the mem
bers had been assured that the Americans
were favorable to their movement.
The two months spent in Jail have had
a terrible effect upon the party. One of
the members is dead and several have be
come Invalids.
The Dominicans are very bitter against
the Americans and declare that their Im
prisonment was worse than any Spanish
outrage. General Wood frequently recom
mended that they be released.. but the au
thorities at Havana prohibited such ac
tion.
Mysterious Death.
MODESTO, Oct. 13.â Jeff Pennington,
aeert 3$ years, a native of Pennsylvania,
died in the County Hospital to-day. He
was found Wednesday morning last wan
dering around near tlie railroad bridge
with a severe scalp wound and several
wounds on his body. Though not fully
rational, he claimed to he from San Fran
cisco, where an uncle resides, and to have
bet a member of the Utah battery in
the Philippine campaign. Coroner Mc-
Mullln will hold at) Inquest to-morrow
afternoon. Death resulted from internal
injuries.

COMMERCIAL AND FINANCIAL NEWS
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver firmer.
Sterling Exchange higher and New York Exchane lower.
Wheat quiet and easy. Barley futures weakened by the rain.
Oats in good demand and firm.
Rye cleaning up and higher. Corn dull.
Hay trading checked by the rain.
Feedstuff firm and unchanged.
Beans continue to rise under speculative purchases.
Rice shows some changes.
No present intention to advance house coals.
Sweet Potatoes and Onions rather firmer.
Vegetables continue to advance.
Butter and Eggs firm and Cheese steady.
Poultry in lighter supply.
Game scarce and selling at fancy prices.
Rain did some damage to Grapes and Berries.
Vine and Table Grapes firm at full figures.
Berries higher and Pears and Lemons lower.
Medium Prunes less weak and selling better.
No change in Provisions.
Meat marl as before quoted.
cased exports of Quicksilver from this port.
Charters.
The Amelia loads Lumber at Port Blakeley

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has risen over the northern half
of the Pacific Slope and has fallen slowly over
Utah, Nevada and Arizona. Rain is reported
from nearly every station in California. At
San Francisco tho rainfall amounted to 1.44 :
inches in the past twenty-four hours, at L*
Angeles 1.08, and equally heavy rainfalls have I
occurred over the roast and valley sections. At !
Fresno a thunder-storm ls reported, with a
rainfall of .84 of an inch. The temperature 1
lias fallen from four to six degrees over the !
greater peirtion of California. It has risen
about eight degrees in Utah and Western Arl
zona: Light frost may be expected ln the north
ern portion of California Sunday morning. A
maximum wind velocity of thirty miles per
hour from the northwest Is reported at Eureka.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours, ending midnight. October 14. l"-99:

continued cool weather; brisk north winds. '

urday morning; fair Saturday; southerly
changing te. north, winds.

day; rain or snow in southern portion.
For Utahâ Rain or snow Saturday.
For Arizonaâ Showers Saturday.
For '-'â ' Francisco and vicinity-Fair Satur- i
day; fresh northwest winds.

EASTERN MARKET
l\ew row stock Market.
NEW YORK, Oct. 13.â The temper of specu
lation was determinedly bearish to-day and
favorable incidents were persistently ignored.
The reactionary spirit found Its chief vent by
selling Brooklyn Transit. That stock was the
leader of the market in point of activity, and
although the dealings lid not approach the
high-water mark of a few weeks ago the un
bupportt-d condition of the stock and the large
blocks offered Â«ug|-estcd a resumption of late
conditions. Metropolitan Street Railway r.cemed
to show acute sympathy. Manhattan was lit
tle affected and the New York Gas stocks were
firm. There were some wide movements in
stocks not usually prominent. New York Air
Brake fell nine points and the Flower group
as a whole, except Rock Island, was weak in
sympathy with Brookly Transit. Offerings of
Sugar and Tobacco were greater than could
bo absorbed and both stocks declined sharply.
The American Malting stocks also were de
pressed, the common losing 2*4 and preferred
7 per cent. The industrials were more affected
than the railways. Laclede â¢ Gas rose con
spicuously 4 points and Great Northern pre
ferred over a point.
The market .seemed rather firm for a time
during the morning and the persistent weak
ness of Brooklyn Transit broke through the
resistance. Call loans were easier again, the
maximum rate not exceeding 64 per cent,
the majority of tha day's loans being made at
1
5 per cent or below. Preliminary estimates of
the week's movements of money give prospect
of a slightly improved condition in to-morrow's
bank statement. The disbursements of cash
by the sub-treasury on account of Government
payments have almost offset its receipts on ac
count of revenues. Put the banks have de
posited at the sub-treasury for telegraphic
transfer to the interior $1,052,000. The heavy
outward movement early in the week has been
partly offset by. large receipts later in the
week. This would Indicate a loss In the in
terior movement of about $2,500,000. Against
this there have been receipts of $2,800,000 of
foreign gold and about $750,000 by cashing of
drafts on the San Francisco mint by the sub
treasury, making a total of about $3,550,000.
This would indicate a net gain to the cash
reserves of the bank of over $1,000,000. The
banks further reduced their loans so there ls
a fair prospect of an increase ln the surplus.
The first payment of anticipated interest on
Government bends will be made on Monday.
But the prospects do not indicate that applica
tion will be male on a large scale, although
this payment Is without rebate. The bond
market was moderately active, but price
changes were mixed. Total sales, par value,
$1,761
United States 3s declined 4 and new 4s ad
vanced 4 in the bid price.

London Market.

vertiser's London financial 'cablegram says:
The markets here are now resinned to war ln
Soutli Africa and loss of gold :in consequence.
They opened irregular, but soon improved
quickly up to the close. African mines were
buoyant. Portuguese threes were again hard.
Spanish fours were 604, Tint is 4.. and Ana
condas a shade under 10. f ;Â».--'
American securities began dull and remained
listless to the close, which -was slack. The.
Cape took Â£300.000 gold and; South America
Â£12,000. Meanwhile the bank bought gold to the
amount of Â£183,000, as follows: i Â£90,000 in bars,
Â£75.000 in German coin and Â£!S,OOO in French
coin. The loss to the Cape was less than had
been expected, th" market counting on an en
gagement of Â£600,000. It Is expected that the
Cape will take half a million Sterling a week
during the war.
Money was slack on the release of Govern
ment dividend, calls being gui"! and bills easy,
the latter, however, only temporarily. Silver
was firm on the home coinage order.

I Bradstrcct's Financial Review.
â¢ , L ; ,
NEW YORK. Oct Bradrtreet's will ay
to-morrow ln its review of the New York
stock market:
The announcement of the Treasury's readi
ness to anticipate all Its Interest 1 , payments for
the current fiscal year contrituied, with small
outside demands for funds and a decreased
pressure for loans on the part of Wall street
Itself, to give the call money market an easy
tone. The. other important development was,
of course, the Transvaal Government's ulti
matum and the. bringing of British and
Boers to open war. The improvement in our
own money market failed, however, to stimu
late speculative activity or to support prices
materially. .The, market continued to be dull,
devoid of outside participation and subject to
professional manipulation for the bear ac
count. In fact, as money became easier these
seemed to be less strength and more aggres
siveness on the part of the bears, leading on
Thursday to an increased downward tendency
in prices, followed en Friday by an increase
of such pressure. There has been no particu
lar evidence of liquidation and the excellent
railroad earnings, the disposition and ability
of the companies to advance rates as well as
the continued activity and prosperity ln the
steel, coal and other industries with which
all street keeps in touch and on which some
of its speculative material is based, produce
a good impression, but mainly as to the fu
ture of prices. For the present the money
market outweighs all other considerations and
the speculative community is possibly not as
fully disposed to exaggerate the bad features
of the financial situation, as it seemed some
weeks ago to ignore such dangers. Declin
ing prices in the face of an improvement in
the tendencies of the New York loan mar
ket and an apparent growth of bearish feel
ing here when London displayed a recovery
in prices and a sense of relief that the un
certainty that the Transvaal situation had
culminated, even If the outcome is war, cer
tainly seems anomalous. But the Inability of
our market to respond may be ln a measure
traced to the fact that unless the Transvaal
war is quickly ended grave disturbances in the
speculative and financial markets abroad
might after all ensue. While money ls easier
in London it has not fallen to the point
where gold can be taken for shipment to
New York. In fact, the Bank of England
will certainly oppose such a movement now.
Large interests here seem unwilling to take
a bullish position in the market while money
is liable to jump to 7 (a 15 per cent should the
market develop any activity and when gold
can neither be imported nor can the return
flow of money taken from New York force
crops moving and business purposes be yet
discerned. The public is also impressed with
the same ideas and a large part of it is
so fully and profitably employed with its own
legitimate concerns that the outside participa
tion in the street is seriously curtailed there
by. The market has, therefore, developed a
growing disposition to wait until the progress
of the South African war affords some idea
of its outcome and duration and until the un
locking of money from the Treasury or the re
ceipts of gold from Europe and currency from
the Interior checks the depletion of the New
York bank reserves and ends the calling of
loans which has been in progress for some
weeks past. This halt in the market and the
insensiblity to th. plentiful favorable facts in
the, situation have naturally led to bearlshness
among the traders and to manipulation de
signed to put prices down.
Bfadstreet's on Trade.
(NEW YORK Oct. 13.â Bradstreefs to-mor
row will say: Trade activity is widespread, all
measures of volume and value testifying to
prevailing prosperous conditions. Only good
reports are received from distributive trad
centers and some markets report fall demand
as holding out longer than expected. Rail
; way earnings, bank clearings returns and
! quotations of staple prices are all encouraging,
pointing as they do to a maximum volume of
business for this period of the year. Crop
returns for October bear out earlier impres
sions of shortened yields of most leading agri
cultural products. Expectation of more mod
erate yields of leading cereals is not confined
to this country, The world's wheat crop will
admittedly be smaller and the yields of rye,
barley and oats are not expected to be bo large
as a year ago. The higher range of prices
of ail staples and particularly of agricultural
products, however, will furnish a profitable
balance to producers. The advance in the
price of cotton has been a notable one ami
though some react n is naturally noted the
effects upon general Southern trade and in
j dustry following the advance are particularly
â  beneficial, as this section alone has appeared
to lag somewhat behind the process of pros
i perity.
In the manufacturing branch of trade
! strength is well nigh universal. Advances
i have been numerous and a scarcity of goods
! rather than of demand is the main subject of
i complaint. While a source of depression to
cotton, the Transvaal situation has proved
rather favoiable to wheat and other cereals,
offsetting heavy increases In the visible sup
pile.-.
The liberality of foreign demand is perhaps
; best known in the September report of ex
â¢ ports of leading products. Shipments of bread
: stuffs wen; as large as those of August and

exports, which are double those of the same
month a year ago. Totals of leading exports
show an Increase of 23 per cent over Septem
ber a year ago and over September, 1896, but
a decrease of 8.5 per cent from September, 1897,
which witnessed very heavy shipments of
breadstuff?.
The general manufacturing industry is run
' ning full and activity is so widespread
i that It Is hard to prefer one industry to an
other In th:.- respect. Coal production is par
' Ocularly heavy, but a scarcity of cars is com.
! plained of in this and in the coke Industry,
; with the strength of lake freights constitut
ing a disturbing element as regards the sup
| ply of anthracite In the West. Iron and steel
| are active as to production, but new business
; is limited by the small quantities available
1 for near-by delivery. Liberal orders are still
reported for the first half of. next year and
steel rails are reported tending upward at the
; West. "â ' --:.-:

out of price cutting. .

Dun's Review of Trade.
NEW YORK. Oct. 13.-R. G. Dun & Co.
weekly review of trade, which issues to-mor
row, will say:
Industrial conditions could hardly be better.
After months of such extraordinary buying
that its continuance seemed Impossible, even
Larger buying still crowds producing works In
most lines beyond their capacity. The iron fur
naces arc producing 1000 tons weekly more than
ever before. 278.615 tons October 1, and yet the
actual consumption in manufacture reduced un
sold stocks In September by 27.380 tons, while
the demand runs far ahead into next year for
many products. After the great buying last
week Bessemer pig rises to $24 at Pittsburg,
with No 1 Anthracite at Philadelphia and No.
1 local coke at Chicago, both quoted at $23 50,
and buyers paying premiums for early deliv
eries according to their needs. Finished prod
ucts still advance. Eastern bar to $2 15 and tank
plates to $3 10. and the average of percentages
to prices of January 1, ISS7. is now 100.64 for
finished products and 105.7 for pig. In some
lines, however, production has so far gained
that works are beginning to look for orders to
cover next year. : â' : , '
The boot and shoe shops are getting a little
better prices for men's kip boots and for
women's grain and buff shoes, and shipments
for the first half of October are the largest
ever reported, though oi-ders for next season
are retarded by prices. Leather ls a shade
higher, but buying of sole has been checked by
the recent advance, as manufacturers appear to
have rather large supplies, and the Chicago
market for hides, though quoted a little high
er, seems uncertain. The demand for woolen
goods is fairly large and prices are well sus
tained, though not further advanced. In goods
of the better grades there Is some uncertainty.
Sales of wool are smaller, only 7.743.300 pounds
for the week at the three markets, without
further advance.
Silks are firm and linens tending upward with
good demand.
The cotton manufacture meets so great a de
mand that prices advance every week and sup- J
piles for early delivery are In many lines re
stricted. More business Is turned down than
is done in brown sheetings and drills and
coarse colored cottons are rising. Big sales
have moved a great deal of the surplus print
cloths, and prices have been advanced for
some grades. The rise of about lc in cot
ton, with current reports of short yield, has
much Influence, and though the quantity com
ing into sight is larger to date than last year,
it has recently been falling behind enough to
support the idea that growers arc disposed to
hold It back.
Wheat exports are still larger than from the
great crop of last year. Atlantic flour included,
having been 7.351.990 bushels in . two weeks,
against 6.116.659 last year, and Pacific. 730,440
bushels, against 1,735,430 last year. The West
ern receipts have been only 14,518.892 bushels,
against 20.790,495 last. year, but the course of
prices is not calculated to encourage a big
movement, for after starting at 75.37 cents and
falling to 75 cents, the market closed at 75c.
with December options unchanged. The Gov
ernment deferred any estimate of yield, and its
figures are as usual variously interpreted, but
net influential.
Failures for the first week of October were.
$1.68.. in amount, manufacturing $387,914 and
trading $408,599.
Failures for the week have been 164 In the
United States, against 250 last year, and 27 in
Canada, against 25 last year. -'â '.-"

Chicago Grain Market.
CHICAGO, Oct. 13.â Wheat opened easy fol
lowing indifferent foreign markets and large
Argentine shipments. The demand was light
and me generous short-selling soon started
the market on the down-grade. About the
middle of the session a report of damage by
drought and insects to the new crop in the
Southwest caused slight rallies, but the mar
ket was soon weighed down again by a re
sumption of short selling anel liquidation, In
duced by steadily increasing stocks all over
the world. There was a Blight upturn near
the close on buying by shorts, who put out
several long lines of Wheat on the early bulges
and took advantage of the decline to buy In
and secure profits, and the market closed with
buyers in the majority. December opened a
shade lower at 72 : _'ii724c, declined to 724 c, rose
to 724 c, sold off again to 72c and closed with
buyers at* 72',c.
Corn ruled active and strong early-, influenced
by higher cables and excellent cash demand.
Liquidation and sympathy with Wheat caused
it to turn weak. December closed at 314 c.
its ruled dull and lower in sympathy with
Wheat and Corn. Longs sold freely. Decem
ber closed at 23c.
Liquidation weakened Provisions early, but
the decline was fully recovered before the
close. The foreign demand for cut meats was
greater than packers were Inclined to 1111 at
nominal prices. Pork and Ribs closed un
changed. January Lard closed a shade lower.
The leading futures ranged as follows:

OREGON.
PORTLAND. Oct. 13.â There has been a dull
wheat market In Portland for the past two
days and sales are few. Ship-owners are mak
ing such extravagant demands for tonnage that
it is a difficult matter to do business without
there is an improvement In the foreign market,
sm farmers will not sell at a figure that would
admit of a profit to the exporters with ships
at 41s 3d to 42s lid and the English cargo mar
ket at 30s. Quotations are nominally ES@s9c
for Walla Walla and 59c for Valley.

Harrison's circular says:
"During the week there have been four deliv
eries of coal from Washington with 13,055 tons;
three from British Columbia, 9634 tons; one
from Oregon. 600 tons; one from Japan, 3700
tons; total. 26989 tons. Our colliery proprietors
are pushing their steamers to their utmost cap
acity, and ere beating records in making fast
time in their deliveries here. The outlook is
improving for our winter supply of fuel, al
though there may not at any time be an ox'er
supply on md, there will be sufficient for cur
rent requirements. There are unfounded ru
mors on the street, which have been repeated
in the papers, that an advance was shortly to
be made on domestic grades for the winter
months. These statements are made to alarm
consumers and force them to lay in large
stocks, whereas at present there is no such in
tention. Present prices will rule for the next
six monthr.
"Freights from foreign ports are hardening,
which means light shipments of coal for several
months to come. Hawaiian Islands are secur
ing all the Australian tonnage for fully eight
months ahead, so that the entire fuel trade is
drifting into the hands of the northern col
lieries,. The transports are relieving us of a
large amount of steam coal, and now that there
is such a Â«cant quantity on hand at Honolulu
i the steamers leaving here are filling their bunk
ers to their full capacity."

OAKLAND WILL
WELCOME THE
TROOPS TO-DAY
â¦ j
Only the Weather Is I
in Doubt.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call. |
90S Broadway, Oct. 13. .
To-morrow's celebration depends en
tirely on the weather. Nobody can con
trol that phase of the situation, and,
therefore, it was decided to-day to go
ahead with the banquet and preparations
and to trust to Providence to do the reiit.
If there should be any sign of a confin
ed . downpour to-morrow morning the
< ration will be postponed to son. day
i.ext week.
Grand Marshal John Mitchell :..:> is
sued his orders tor the parade. Tin- [ire;
in brief, that the parade will "si.ut
promptly from Fifth and Broad wa.'. at
10:30 o'clock, and march up Broad v.. .â¢>
to Washington, to Fourteenth. .â¢'
Broadway to Bevel I â  Â»rmarch to
Fourteenth, to Han Twelfth, to
Webster. The gram, will review
the parade at Font:. : -..i Webster.
The parade will con. . the National
Guard. Uniformed Hank, Veteran Re
serve; carriages with Invited guests. In
cluding General Shafter and staff, Briga
dier Generals Funston and Kessler, Gov
ernor Stanley and staff of Kansas and
Senator George C. Perkins.
The Kansas and Montana regiments
will be accompanied by their Own bands.
About seventy-five ladies have been
working all da at the Reliance Club
hulldlng on the preparation for the ban
quet. These workers are from the Red
Cross Society, the Lyon Relief Corps, Ap
pom:.' i Corps. Daughters of the Revo
lution, Lad of the Grand Army, Jewish
Ladies' Relief Society and the Native
Daughters.
Mrs. I. L. Requa and Mrs. W. T. Veitch
were the commanding generals of the
sandwich-making army, and â¢':.<-â  tactics
displayed In keeping their detachments of
willing workers turning out exquisite
sandwiches shows that they have reduced
sandwich-making to a science.
The grand marshal has issued his final
word as follows: "Again permit me to
say to the people, let the streets be lined
with an enthusiastic, cheering multitude.
and let the welcome extended be of such
a nature as to instantly convince these
veteran soldier boys that they are ln the
midst of friends, eagerly anxious to show
In every way that the gallant defenders
of Old Glory, who have carried our loved
flag through many a fierce fight, always
forward and to victory, have won our ad
miration and gratitude."

A SUCCESSFUL PERFORMANCE.
Young People of Mission Churches j
Produce a Charming Operetta.
The operetta "Golden Hair and the I
Three Bears" was produced last evening I
at ilinman Hall, Twenty-first and Mis- j
sion streets, by the young people of the |
Mission churches. The "fair was under j
'theT Ladies' ; ;. Auxiliary of the Mission j
branch of the Young Men's Christian As- j
socJation, and its purpose was to raise
funds to furnish the auxiliary's new quar
tors at 7 and 11 Hill street.
The performance was one of exceptional '.
merit, and Mrs. L. Straub. who had j
charge of the rehearsals, deserves much j
credit for the excellent manner in which i
the young participants acquitted them- j
selves'. The operetta developed a youth- "
ful prodigy in the person of little Miss I
Adelaide Killgore, who sang the part of
Qolden Hair. She is scarcely 12 years old, !
yet possesses a rich contralto voice that !
was a surprise to every one who heard
her sing. She handled her voice like a
veteran and acted her part like a profes- !
Elonal. All who took part deserve praise i
for their excellent work. The chorus of I
fifty little tots was a feature of the per
form;,

ATHLETES OF WEST
OAKLAND PROSPER
OAKLAND, Oct. IS.â Athletics are to be boomed in West Oakland. The
West Oakland Athletic Club has just received its certificate of Incor
poration from Sacramento, and is now prepared to enter upon an active
career. Nearly a year ago the club was originally created, but It has
rapidly outgrown the anticipations of Its founders, who have found it
necessary to place it on a substantial footing. ; ' â 
George W. Bastian has been elected president, which office he filled before
the Incorporation. The other officers are: Frank Simpson, first vice president;
C. L. Wines, treasurer; Frank Hufschmidt, secretary. George Simpson, who
ls to box twenty rounds with Charley Tye at the Acme Club next week, is
the boxing instructor, and Charles (onion has been engaged for instructor
in wrestling. The directors are: James Drew, George Simpson, William Hal
loran. Silas Lumsden. Al Fie!.. William Duphrau, E. W. Kuster, George W.
Bastian, Frank Simpson, C. L. Wines and Fred Hufschmidt.
The club has a membership of seventy-five, and ten new applications were
received at the last meeting. The clubrooms are well fitted up, and it has
been found that the. rooms can be well equipped and maintained by levying
dues at $1 per month. At the present time the club is entirely out of debt and
has $400 in its treasury-
In addition to the athletic features the social side of club life has not
been neglected, and there are also well-supplied music and card rooms.
Wednesday and Friday have been set apart as class nights, and there are al
ways about thirty members stripped, ready for tuition, on these occasions. In
the near future the club will present some fine athletic programmes.

west of th.- Rocky Mountains and it is
to I .' held under the auspices of the San I
Francisco Hussars. At a meeting of the
Hussars 'ast night the programme for a
celebration was outlined and committees [

ous matters pertaining thereto. The cele
bration, which li scheduled to take place
on November 4, will consist of a night ;
parade, followed by a grand entertain
ment and ball In Mechanics' Pavilion.
State and Federal officials will be invit
ed to attend the exercises and Invitations |
to deliver addresses will be extended to I
the Mayor, Congressman Kahn, Senator |
Perkins, Rabbi Voorsanger and other well i
known orators. '
FOR A BRANCH MUSEUM.
Merchants' Association to Establish
a Permanent Exhibit Here.
Several months ago The Call announced
that the Merchants' Association of this
city proposed to establish in San Fran
cisco a branch of the Philadelphia Com
mercial Museum. Manager Wilson of the
Eastern body promised to donate to the
Western branch many of the exhibits, of
which there is a surplus. A number of
the members of the local body have been
East, and their investigation of the mat
ter has led them to become enthusiasti
cally favorable to the project. This Inter
est is shown by the following, which ap
pears In the forthcoming issue of the Re
view, the official organ of the associa
tion:
The Philadelphia Commercial Mu
seum is now holding a magnificent x
position, that has excited the atten
tion and admiration of every commer
cial center of the world. The products
of all lands are extensively repre
sented there. So successful and valu
able has the Philadelphia Commercial
Museum proven that other prominent
cities are about to start similar In
stitutions. Not to be outdone by Phil
adelphia, the Merchants' Association
of New York has decided to in
augurate In the metropolis a perma
r.ent commercial museum upon a still
more extensive scale. Considerable
correspondence has been exchanged
between our association and the Phila
delphia Commercial Museum upon the
establishment of a branch museum In
San Francisco. Many convincing ar
guments favor such an enterprise ln
this Western metropolis. Its peculiar
advantages to our merchants and
manufacturers, particularly in view of
thi- opening of Oriental markets,
should bring about the establishment
of a San Francisco Commercial Mu
seum at an early date. Every mer
cantile and commercial organization
should unite In consummating this in
dispensable institution for trade ex
pansion.
POKED HIS EYE OUT.
William Taylor Uses the Point of an
Umbrella With Disastrous
Result. *
William Taylor, a colored man 21 years
of age, was arrested yesterday morning
and booked at the City Prison on a
charge of mayhem. The complaining
witness is J. J. Tompkins, a horse
trainer from Chicago, living at the
Golden Gate Hotel.
Tompkins was standing outside the ho
tel shortly after midnight talking to
friends when Taylor approached them
and began to talk to them. Tompkins
told him to go away, and when Taylor
asked why Tompkins , replied: "Don't
like niggers, nohow." Taylor had an
umbrella in his hand and made a lunge
with It at Tompkins" face and the point
struck his left eye.
Tompkins was taken to the Receiving
Hospital and Dr. Dray attended the in
jured eye. He said that the night was
entirely gone.

CARELESSNESS CHARGED
Excelsior Planing-Mill Blamed for
the Death of Martin Shaugh
nessy, an Employe.
Coroner Hill held an Inquest yesterday
upon the body of Martin Shaughnessy.
who died ln St. Mary's Hospital on Octo
ber 10 from the effect of Injuries received
whiie at work in the planing mill of the
Excelsior Redwood Company. The testi
mony showed that Shaughnessy, while
doing some work with the ripsaw, picked
up a piece of wood from the floor and
changed the belt from one pulley to an
other with it. The belt was running at
the time, and the stick was twisted in
such a way as to strike Shaughnessy in
the stomach
The jury returned a verdict that the de
ceased met his death by an accident
caused by gross carelessness of his em
ployer. The apparatus for shifting the
belt was out of order and could not be
used, and Shaughnessy was obliged to use
the stick instead. i
PUBLISHERS' BUILDING
AT THE PARIS FAIR
CHICAGO, Oct. 13.â Commissioner Gen
eral Peck has just awarded the contract
for a most Important United States build
ing at the Paris Exposition of 1900. The
structure will be known as the Publish
ers' building and will be located in the
new (or Esplanade dcs Invalidcs) section
of the exposition grounds. In It will be
made the displays of American printing
machinery and supplies.
In this building will be the general
headquarters or rendezvous for United
States publishers. Here publishers will be
made to feel at home amid familiar en
vironments.
The Publishers' building belongs to the
department of liberal arts, of which A.
S. Capebart Is director, and the main
building will be under the charge of
Charles Harris Simms, assistant director
of liberal arts.
Newspaper and other publishers are In
vited to send their publications to these
headquarters during the exposition, and
those who intend to visit the exposition
next summer are requested to notify the
liberal arts department of such fact, in
order that they may be registered and
certified to the proper French officials

ALAMEDA COUNTY
TO PRISON FOR
A YEAR BECAUSE
OF THIRTY CENTS
Yon Kapff a Victim of
Fee System.
Oakland Office Ran Francisco Call.
DOS Broadway. Oct. 13.
Henry W. yon Kapff, ex-constable of
Alameda, who was recently convicted of
having sworn falsely to affidavits at
tached to his claims for fees, was sen
tenced by Judge Hall to-day to one year's
Imprisonment in San Quentln. a motion
for a new trial having been previously de
nied. By stipulation it was agreed that
the making out of the commitment be de
ferred for another week to give Yon Kapff
time in which to appeal to the Supreme
Court, but it is believed that he does not
desire to appeal. /> .
Before pronouncing sentence Judge Hall
took occasion to criticize the system of
paying fees to petty officers. He noted
the fact that Yon Kapff while in offlce had
about fifteen deputies.
"This number.'' said his Honor, "was
not required to attend to the ordinary
business that could come to his offlce, and
consequently it was hard for deputies to
make fees. The result is that the young
man finds himself in this court as a con
vict, having perjured himself for a few
dimes."
Yon Kapff. who at one time was one of
the most popular young men in Alameda,
appeared crestfallen when sentence was
pronounced. He feels bitterly the stigma
that the proceedings attach to his name.
HENRY W. YON KAPFF.
and he still protests his Innocence.
"I am an innocent victim of circum
stances," he said as he once more entered
1 the prison corridor. "I think the verdict
] of the jury an unjust one. as I never in
tentionally violated any law. Even the
I Judge remarked that there was no real i
; criminal Intent, but the jury rendered its |
; verdict otherwise, and his Honor gave me
the minimum sentence. I believe. It's a
case of punishing a man on general prin
ciplesâthat constables In this county have
held up the treasury for years past.
"I'm convicted of overcharging the
county the exorbitant sum of 30 cents.
Whether deliberately or otherwise, I j
overcharged this amount', and this, too,
during a period of four years in offlce. ;
The presumption is great that a man
with no stain upon his character, having
a young wife, an aged father and mother
and sisters to mourn his loss, would not
deliberately commit suicide, so to speak,
for this great amount of money. -It seems
to me that It Is a constable that they
wanted to punish, no matter what his
name, and in the drawing of straws,
which I had no hand In, I'm the one that |
ls to die." ___
GAMMA ETA KAPPA
WILL CONVENE TO-DAY
OAKLAND, Oct. 13.â State conven
tion of the Gamma Eta Kappa Fraternity
will meet in Pythian Hall, on Twelfth
street, to-morrow afternoon at 2 o'clock.
The fraternity has chapters only in High
Schools, and delegates will attend from
Oakland, San Francisco, San Jose, Stock
ton, Santa Cruz, San Bernardino, Los
Angeles, Portland and Denver. In the
evening a banquet will be served at the
Hotel Metropole, when Attorney Ben L.
Woolner will act as toast master.
A Woman Burglar.
OAKLAND. Oct. 13.â A woman giving
the name of Mrs. Frizelle was arrested
to-day by Detectives Shorey and Holland.
She has been regarded with suspicion for
some time, going about from house to
house under the name of Macdonald and
claiming to peddle corn salve. She is now
charged with having entered the residence
of Mrs. W. Murphy, 523 Eighth street, and
carrying off considerable clothing and
small ornaments, which have since been
identified by the owner. The officers be
lieve that the woman may be responsible
for a number of recent burglaries com
mitted in hotel rooms.

BERKELEY, Oct. 13.â The freshmen of
the University of California lined up on
the college football field this afternoon
for a match game against Boone's Uni
versity Academy of Berkeley. The con

game will be played out at some other
date. _____________

Anand A. Advani. the alleged Indian
merchant, who has been swindling banks
In. different parts of the world, was re
leased from custody by Chief Lees Thurs
day night, but on the condition that he
would report at police headquarters every
morning till he leaves the city. He re
ported yesterday morning and consented
to have his photograph taken. The Chief
is satisfied with the publicity given to
Advanl's operations, which will warn
bankers to be on their guard, and will
not prosecute the swindler. â  ,--Vc" :â ;?::â :

THE GOAT COST ONE
DOLLAR PER HOUR
OAKLAND. Oct. 13.â J. J. Carter bought a goat last Saturday. When
he got home the goat died and to-day Prosecuting Attorney Leach. Dep
uty District Attorney Samuels and Assistant City Attorney Condon were
studying the statutes to find out if a man who sold a goat which died
two hours after the sale and who represented said goat to be thoroughly
healthy could 1 c prosecuted for obtaining money under false pretenses.
Mr. Carter appeared at the City Hall to-day with his tale of the goat.
He told the legal gentlemen that at the free market last Saturday he pur
chased Mr. William from an unknown dealer. The goat was frisky and ap
peared to be a very lively animal. In fact. It tried to use Its horns on the
way to the Carter home. Mr. Carter paid $2 for the goat and two hours
after he bought It William died. Mr. Carter seriously Informed the prosecut
ing attorney that $1 per hour for '.he possession of a goat was direct fraud
and he asked for the assistance of a police officer and a John Doe ** arrant
to go and secure the arrest of the goat merchant. Attorney Samuels uejllncd
to issue. the warrant, stating that In his opinion buying goats was something
like swapping horsesâ you have to take your chance. Mr. Carter left the
City Hall totally dissatisfied that a citizen of Oakland could have no redress
from the courts after being so grievously imposed upon in the purchase of
a goat.

TROUT WERE POISONED
OR ELSE ELECTROCUTED
OAKLAND, Oct. Three hundred !
dead speckled trout are floating on the
beautiful lake in the Schilling grounds.
Two days ago all the fish were lively,
but the next morning all were dead. Mr.
Schilling is at a loss to account for the
death of his fish and at firs', thought that
some poison must have been thrown Into
the pond. The Schilling grounds, how
ever, are always open to the public ex
cept at auch times as Mr. Schilling de
sires to keep them private, and no one
can conceive of any reason why a vandal
should wish to poison the beautiful fish.
It Is possible that a livÂ«- electric wire
may have got Into the pon- for the Schil
ling grounds are a perfei I network of
electric wires, used for ill' nlnatlng pur
poses. One of the trout ar- a sample if
the water from the pond h ye been given
to the analyst at the State University and
in a few days it will be ascertained
whether the fish died from poison or
whether the pond charged with electricity
caused the electrocution of the trout.

Charles C. Sullivan will be held before

Sure's courtroom. Sullivan was removed

prisoner was taken by Marshal Conrad
and Officer Anderson to the County Jail
in Oakland

victs. It was only last Friday that he
visited the place and waited the arrival
of' her son. Mrs. Willmore declares that
owing to Sullivan's bad Influence she had
forbidden his calling at her house.

LABOR UNIONS
WILL WORK FOR
LOCAL PROGRESS
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Oct. 13.
The presidents of the various trade or
ganizations in this city are arranging a
mass-meeting for next Thursday evening
at Gier's Hall. The purpose of the meet
ing is to further the campaign of the
Merchants' Exchange to increase the local
trade of this city and to prevent It cross
ing the bay. The meeting will not be con
fined to organized labor, but the opera
tives of all manufactures and industries
of Oakland. At the same time a recep
tion will be tendered to Samuel B. Don
nelly, grand president of the International
Typographical Union, who will also make
an address.
A. J. Reid, ex-president of the State
Typographical Union; M. A. Mclnnes,
president of the Oakland Typographical
Union; C. D. Rogers, district organizer of
the American Federation of Labor; M. L.
Schnitzer of the Oakland Tribune, James
Shields of the Berkeley Gazette, Reuben
Wland of the Brotherhood of Carpenters
and Joiners and T. F. Feeney, secretary
of the Cigar-makers' Union, are charged
with -the duty of appointing sub-com
mittees to perfect the details of the mass
meeting and reception.
A conference was held between these
gentlemen and M. M. Barnett of the Mer
chants' Exchange this morning, and
enough assurance was given to satisfy all
that the mass-meeting will be a great
success.

WIDOW CHARGES
THAT F. C. SIEBE.
DELUDED HER
Mrs. Petermann Files
a Queer Suit.
Oaklr ad Office San Francisco Call.
90S Broadway, Oct. 13.
A rather sensational suit was filed in
the Superior Court this afternoon by Mrs.
Mary Petermann of Mount Eden against
Fred C. Siebe of San Francisco, her de
ceased husband's former business part
ner, wherein she makes grave charges of
having been duped by him out of a con
siderable portion of the $100,000 estate left
to her and eight children by her husband.
Henry Peterraann, who died in April, 1891
The complaint recites that shortly after
the husband's death a son. Henry Peter
mann Jr., engaged In business and spec
ulative enterprises, borrowing large sums
from Siebe, in whom the widow had re
posed implicit confidence and who had
voluntarily offered his advice and counsel
pertaining to her property. Young Peter
mann sustained heavy losses and in 1894
was Indebted to Siebe for $15,000. Mrs.
Petermann, however, had no knowledge
of such transactions until Siebe presented
the notes to her for indorsement, long
after the same became due, but still hav
ing full confidence in Slebe's honesty, she
signed the notes, Siebe advising her that
it would not be necessary for her to con
sult an attorney In the matter.
Later, continues the complaint, and
while young Petermann was insolvent,
Siebe loaned $1000 more and took a note
for the same, which she never Indorsed,
and on October 2, 1896, defendant blandly
informed her that "she was now indebted
to him in the sum of $23,000 and that in
order to secure payment of the same it
would be necessary for her to execute
mortgages upon certain of her realty lv
favor of Siebe." She then executed two
mortgages, one for $20,000 on Alameda
County property and the other for $3000
on San Francisco property.
"All of which plaintiff alleges was dona
without any consideration and that de
fendant wickedly and falsely misrepre
sented to her that she owed him $23,000
for the purpose of defrauding and cheat
ing her out of her property."
Later Mrs. Petermann was prevailed
upon by Siebe to surrender control of her
property, so It appear in the complaint,
and finally, in April, 1896, she was pre
vailed upon to make a deed. The plain
tiff further alleges that in taking control
of her property Siebe also collected notes
she had against various parties for over
$20,000, none of which he gave her credit
for, and that on September 27, 1899, de
fendant claimed she owed him $34,637, and
requested a trust deed for all her prop
erty, threatening if she refused he would
foreclose the mortgages.
The widow asserts that she never owed
Siebe over $2000. She asks that the court
direct Siebe to render an accounting and
that the mortgages given by her to him
be declared null and void.

REALTY TORE CONTINUES TO IMPROVE
THE tone of the real .estate market
continues- to improve, and. brokers
almost without exception predict a
prosperous business for October.
There is plenty of money seeking invest
ment, and the only drawback now ls de
sirable offerings. Owners appear to be
unwilling to part with their holdings or
are asking too much for their properties.
Any one with a desirable piece of busi
ness property, improved or unimproved,
should have no difficulty in disposing of
the same at a fair figure. *:-.
Business has been fairly good during
the past week, although no new sales
involving large amounts have been re
ported. Confirmation has been received,
however, of several sales of goodly pro
portions heretofore referred to in these
columns. Among the latter is the sale
of the Ehrman property, on Mission
street, near Fourth, 50x100, for $70,750.
Douglas S. Watson has purchased the
three-story building and lot 25x00 on
the northwest corner of Powell and Post
streets of Mrs. Rebecca Jennings for
$50,000; also the adjoining property, con
i sisting of a lot 23xG0 and a two-story
! and basement building from Julia Law-
I ton, Lily Asher and J. Radston for $30,
--| 000. Boardman, Hooper & Co. negotiated
i the sales. The same firm also reports
the following sales: Lot on the north
' west side of Howard street, 412:6 north
: east of Sixth .street, 28:6 frontage on
Howard and 62:6 feet on Natoma, .with a
depth of 80 feet, lodging-house, stores
and flats, sold for Mrs. W. E. Cook for
$16,500; the George A- Carr residence and

Race for a Claim.
SALINAS, Oct. 13.â The town of Hollis
ter, as well as the surrounding country,
has the oil fever. Some miners at New
Idria quicksilver' mines struck an oil
seepage with a pick. Immediately the
claim was staked and located by two
parties and a rush was made to the coun
ty seat to have the claim recorded. Mr.
Rnmires and J. M. Coulson traveled all
night from the mines, reaching Hollister
at .an early hour, each supposing the
other to be behind him. Coulson found the
Recorder at 8:90 and hurried him to the
offlce, only to find that the other man had
procured a Deputy Recorder and filed his
claim ten minutes previously.
Farley's Estate.
SALINAS, Oct. Judge M. Farley,
father of the murdered Sheriff of this
county, has been appointed as adminis
trator of his son's estate. The estate is a
valuable one.

chants' Exchange, San Francisco, Cal ,
October 13, 1-W.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferrry
building was dropped at exactly noon to-dayâ
I. c., at noon of the 120 th meridian, or at 8
o'clock p. m,, Greenwich time.
C. G. CALKINS,

Sun, Moon and Tide.
United States Coast and Geodetic Survey-
Times and Heights of High and Low
Waters at Fort Point, entrance to San
Francisco Bay. Published by official au
thority of the Superintendent.
NOTEâ The high and low waters occur at
the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-five minutes later than at Fort Point;
the height of tide is the same at both places.
SATURDAY, OCTOBER 14.
NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights
given are in addition to the sound-ngs on the
United . States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

ple's Party â Dictator McNab's
County Committee As
sembles.
Citizens will assemble at Metropolitan
Hall this evening to ratify the ticket
placed in the field by the Republican
Municipal Convention. Hon. Horace
Davis, the able and distinguished nominee
for Mayor, will address the meeting.
Other speakers of ability will also enter
tain and instruct the audience.
The Republicans enter the local contest
â¢with a firm determination to achieve suc
cess by appealing to the Intelligence and
civic patriotism of the great city of San
Francisco. Prominent men who have
hitherto paid but little attention to elec
tion contests other than those of national
character are coming forward at the pres
ent time pledging their energies to pro
mote the success of the Republican mu
nicipal ticket as th means of securing
tjood government lor the residents of San
_-'ran Cisco.
Old timers and young men alike will
Join in giving Horace 'avis cheering rec
cgnitlon. He has rendered the working
men, the merchants, the bankers, the ed
ucators, the boys and the girls of this
town so many honorable and helpful ser
vices during the past forty years that all
Will be glad to give him the hand of
friendship and wish him success. Horace
Davis in a man of the plain people, whose
lot in life was cast ami ; the I tilers;
hence it is not singular that he Is a man
of human sympathies. He is not what
â¢ the rich and smart set would term a bril
liant man, but he is sincere, honest and
truthful, possessing a fund of sound Com
mon sense useful to the city, and more
to bo prized in a public servant than
flashes of oratorical genius.
The Horace Davis Central Republican
Club headquarters. History building, 723
Market street, Is daily gaining large ac
cessions of members. Republicans ip
preclate the fact that they I i+ve a laud
light ahead of them, but confidence in
their ability to Win is hourly increasing.
The merchants are particularly active tn
the cause of good government Yester
day the finance committee of the cam
paign was organized. Fourteen leading
men of the city wire Invited to meet at
the office of Henry T. Scott on Market
street, and not a man whose services on
the committee were sought failed to re
spond. Money necessary for the legiti
mate conduct of the campaign will doubt
less be contributed by the enterprising
taxpayers of the community. At the
meeting were: Irving M. Scott, George
... Newhall, John F. Merrill. W. H.
Crocker, P. N. LiHenthal. Colonel William
Macdonald, Mr. Sic-. of Miller, Sloss &
Pcott, and others. The committee elected
TV. li. Crocker treasurer.
The committee appointed by the Repub
lican convention to fill vacancies on the
ticket will meet to-day. Only two vacan
cies are to be filled. Mr. Koster, whose
name was placed on the ticket for Super
visor without his consent, declined to ac
cept for the reason that business affairs
would not Justify his acceptance. I. J.
Truman, treasurer, will be placed on the !
ticket, to fill the first vacancy. Joseph
Britton accepted a nomination bill de
cided after reflection to withdraw. When
he accepted he had not fully weighed the
proposition, but had simply "hefted" It
The story goes that Pheian persuaded
him to retire and wrote a letter which
lias since placed one or both in a bad
plight Emmet Barrett, a sterling man
of worth and character, will be placed on
the ticket to fill this vacancy. The com
mittee had a list of fifty acceptable men ]
for each place. Mr. Koster. whose dec
lination was regretted, is in full sympathy
with the Republican movement for reform
in municipal affairs and will give Horace
Davis earnest support
The new Republican County Com
mittee will meet at 723 Market street at
7:30 o'clock this evening and proceed to
the election of a chairman and secre
tary. The session will be brief, as the
members will desire to attend the rati
fication meeting at Metropolitan Hall.
Delegates Meakln, Kinno and Walsh,
comprising a committee from the People's
party convention, waited upon Senator R
Porter Ashe yesterday and urged him not
to decline the party's nomination for
Mayor. The Populists are confident of
polling a very large vote. It is claimed
that thousands of Democrats of the
Bryan following will not recognize the
McNab convention, but on the contrary
will acknowledge the People's party as
the regular Democratic organization.

County Committee Re-Elects
All Its Former Officers.

gether last night at B'nai B'rith Hall on
Eddy street and effected permanent or
ganization. M.- Jasper McDonald was
elected to succeed himself as chairman
and a like honor was done Thomas J.

elected treasurer by acclamation, upon
motion of E. I. Coffey.
Mr McDonald was placed in nomination
by Emll Pohll in an intricate but well
executed speech. He spoke of his nomi
nee's long and good service in the party
and was frequently applauded. Andrew
J. Maguire offered Mr. Walsh's name and
was as happy as was Pohli in his Intro
ductory remarks. Both candidates were
elected by acclamation.

SAN FRANCISCO WAS AT
THE MERCY OF THE IOWA
For the First Time in the History of the Port a Warship Clears for
Action*
FOR half an hour yesterday San Francisco lay at
the mercy of a ship-of-war. The big fighting ma
chine lowa was cleared for action and the shotted
guns were trained upon the city. The officers were
all at their posts, the range had been found, the
gunners stood to their guns, and all that was needed was
the word of command to lay the Queen City of the Pacific
in ruins. instead of the order to commence firing, how
ever, 'the cartridges were withdrawn from the guns, the
deck hamper was restored to its place, the lifeboats were
recalled to the ship and in an hour from the time prepara
tions began everything was going along on board the
warship as though such a thing as a sea fight had never
been heard of.
The boys on the lowa have not forgotten how to clear
their ship for action. The speed with which they got her
ready for a brush with the Spanish fleet was noted at the
time, but yesterday, in the piping days of peace, they
equaled if not excelled that record. Soon after breakfast
the order was given to put the ship in fighting trim. AH
the lifeboats and launches were sent over to the cruiser
Marblehead; the wire netting around the ship was taken
down; the bridge was removed; the big cranes that hoist
the steam launches inboard were lowered; the yard on the
pole that surmounts the fighting top was taken down and
stowed away, the fighting tops were manned, and in the
I the primaries with any delegates from his i
I own district, Senator Sammy Braunhart
took a large and conspicuous part in the ,
I evening's programme and was handed a
committee on rules of five members as hw
reward. Chairman McDonald promised
to submit the names at a future date.
Louis de F. Bartlett moved the appoint
ment of a campaign committee, one mem
ber to be selected from each Assembly ;
District. His motion carried and the'
chairman made a similar announcement
Then there were loud cries of "Popper.
"Popper," from the back of the hall, and
Max of that name got up to hand out a
few points on "How J would conduct this
campaign if I were younger." Mr. Pop
per's style and delivery had suffered noth
ing by long disuse and his speech was the
feature of the entertainment. In opening
he gracefully presented himself with sev
eral handsome floral designs and gave off
to his hearers the comforting assurance
that the coming campaign was to be thÂ«
easiest thing although the hardest that
Democracy ever got to windward of, and
that although already won every one
would have to fight hard to make the
campaign a success. Continuing, he said
that Horace Davis could not and should
not be elected, because he had never read
the funding bill, and he then pointed with
I pride to the fact that Mayor Pheian had
not only road it but sent telegrams to
every Congressman in Washington con
cerning it. Mr. Popper's suggestions were
received with loud applause, which was ,
only interrupted by a motion by some one
in the back of the hall to appoint a com
mittee of five to purge the Great Register j
"for the benefit of the party." The mover
offered no suggestions as to how this
scheme could be worked, so it was re- :
ferred to the campaign committee for con- i
sideration. Adjournment was taken to j
the call of the chair.

The County Committee of the Citizens' :

MRS. KOPP MUST
BE TRIED OVER AGAIN
; The Jury Disagrees as to Her Guilt in
Shooting Her Husband â
to One for Acquittal.
The Jury in whose hands rested the fate
I of Mrs. Louisa Kopp, charged with man-
I slaughter for having shot her husband on
June 28 last, disagreed last night and was
discharged. it is understood that the
members stood eleven to one for acquittal.
Judge Ferral, Mrs. Kopp's .attorney,
managed by tact to work up an incident
; that doubtless wavered the jury In the
â¢ prisoner's favor. Mrs. Bertha Monti, the
woman who it is alleged was responsible
for tin; shooting of Kopp by reason of her
relations with him, was in the courtroom
and took a seat near the defendant. As
sistant District Attorney Greany, it was
expected, had concluded to call Mrs.
Monti as a witness in rebuttal to prove
that the allegation of her intimacy with
the dead was not based on fact. He failed
to call her, however, and after the case
had rested Judge Ferral stated that all
witnesses would now be excused. Mrs.
Monti kept her seat despite the fixed
stare of the attorney, and then Judge Fer
ial, seeing that she was not easily fright
ened, asked the court to read the order
allowing all witnesses to retire. Again
Judge Ferral turned and fixed his eyes on
Mrs. Monti and this time she lost her
nerve. Hastily rising from her seat she
fled the room and was not seen again. The
Incident caused considerable comment and
all in favor of the accused.

NOTORIOUS BRASS THIEF.
Charles McDevitt, Alias "the Monk,"
Arrested on a Felony Charge.
Charles McDevitt, alias "The Monk." an
ex-convict and all-around thief, who has
for the past fifteen years been a thorn in
the flesh of the special officers of the
Southern Pacific Company, is in trouble
again.
He was released from the County Jail
Thursday morning after serving a term
of thirty days for stealing brass from the
Southern Pacific machine shops, the
charge having been reduced from burg
lary to petty larceny. In the evening be
THE SA3ST Ay CISCO CALL, SATURDAY, OCTOBER 14, 1899.
incredibly short space of half an hour there was nothing
left for; the enemy to aim at but the armored hull, the
fighting mast and the smokestacks.
incredibly short space of half an hour there was nothing
left for the enemy to aim at but the armored hull, the
fighting mast and the smokestacks.
It took Just half an hour to get the battleship back
into her old condition, and then life went on aboard the
lowa as though nothing had happened. The men worked
like machines and there was not a hitch from the moment
the drill began until It was finished.
"The beauty of it all." said one of the officers, "Is that
the boys act just the same in a fight as they do in a drill.
There was no excitement when we were getting ready for
the work cut out for us off the coast of Cuba, and there
was no excitement to-day when we were clearing the
ship for action. The American sailor Is the greatest and
coolest fighting machine the world has ever seen."
The charing of the lowa for battle yesterday was the
first time in the history of the port that such a drill has
taken place. Hundreds of warships have lain peacefully
at anchor in the bay, but never before has one stowed all
the emblems of peace and taken on the visage of grim
war. Stripped of all her deck hamper, the lowa looked
what she isâ a terrible engine of destruction.
During the afternoon Major General Shafter paid a
friendly sit to the captain of the battleship. As It was
not an official event no salute was fired when the general
left the lowa.
ARNOLD MEYER KILLED
BY TOUCHING A LIVE WIRE
WHILE engaged In decorating
Market street ln honor of the
Golden Jubilee of the Odd Fel
lows, Arnold Meyer, ; aged 19
years, who was employed by
Charles Goldberg, a decorator at 624 Ellis
street, was almost Instantly killed last
evening by coming in contact with a live
wire. In his hurry to complete the deco
rations Meyer thoughtlessly grabbed the
death-dealing wire and received! a shock
that resulted In his death a few minutes
later. .', ;
Shortly before 6 o'clock, Meyer, with
two assistants, started to stretch a string
of flags across Market street, near Sixth.
The unfortunate young man entered the
building at 1007*_ Market street, and,
standing on the fire escape, he called to
his fellow workers to pass the 1 string of
flags to him. Ills object was to fasten one
end to the fire escape and the other to
the building on the opposite side; of the
street. While adjusting the string Meyer
grabbed hold of the deadly wire for sup
port, j 1
The shock was so great that : he was
hurled to the bottom of the fire! escape.
Ills agonizing shriek attracted the atten
tion of F. H. Brans, who has |an office
in the building, and he rushed to the un
fortunate man's assistance. Me was
quickly picked up and carried Into Bruns'
office. A few minutes later he (breathed
his last and his body was taken to the
Morgue. i '
broke into the machine shops at Fourth
and Townsend streets and stole six brass
journal bearings. He was caught in the
act by Special Detective Lewin and Spe
cial Officer Madden of the company and
charged at the City Prison with felony
petty larceny, second offense. j i
McDevitt appeared before Judge Conlan
yesterday, and by consent the case was
continued till Monday. Every effort will
be made to send him across; the bay
again. _ â¢ '-..

The San Francisco Public School Teach

Bruns, the dentist, was seated in his

the accident happened.
"Meyer asked my permission to go
through my ofllce to reach the fire es
cape," he remarked when telling the
story cf the accident. "As I had no ob
jection he opened the window and climbed
on to the fire escape. His two assistants
quickly followed him, and while he as
cended the stairs of the fire escape they
remained on the lower platform, ready to
render any assistance he desired. Just as
he had fastened the string of flags to the
fire escape he suddenly threw up his
hands and uttering a piercing shriek fell
to the bottom, his head striking against
the Iron steps.
"We hurriedly picked him up and car
ried him into my offlce. A short time
afterward he dud."
Meyer, in addition to being a decorator,
was an extra man in the Fire Depart
ment.
After the body had been taken to the
Morgue an affecting scene occurred there,
which brought tears to the eyes of the
spectators. The mother of the boy and
his uncle. Martin Aronson, both of whom
are deafmutes, viewed the remains, and
their demonstrations of sorrow by signs
were pitiful in the extreme. The unfortu
nate boy was the only support of his
mother and five brothers.

COUNSEL WHO

Attorney Bowden Was
His Victim.
